ADJUNTAS GIVES
HEARTY WELCOME
TO AMERICANS
Invading Forces in Porto Rico
Regarded as the Island's
Deliverers.
Special cable to The Call and the NÂ«?w York
Herald. Copyrighted.. IS'JS.- by James Gor
don Bennett.
IPONCE, Porto Rico, Aug. 2 (by Call-
Herald dispatch boat to St. Thomas, D.
W. 1., Aug. 3).â American troops under
General Stone have been given a hearty
welcome to Adjuntas. Members of
the Fire Department there have
been armed by the Americans andnow
are acting as a local police force.' There
Is a disposition among the country peo
ple to torture the Spaniards, but the
Americans will not tolerate such a
course. . ; ; â 
In Juan Diaz and Cnamo the officials
have greeted the Invading forces as de
liverers, and American flags have been
raised over the principal buildings in
both places, .
IÂ£ is. General Stone's plan to advance
from Adjuntas to the northern coast of
Porto Rico as rapidly as possible. He
is making, his way toward Areclbo, in
the northern part of the island, at
which point the Spaniards have been
landing supplies, for there has been a
blockade of Areclbo and the Spaniards,
taking advantage of that fact, have
been landing supplies there without
hindrance. A railroad extends from
Arecibo to San Juan, and by this
method it has been possible to keep the
capital well supplied with all neces
sary articles. General Stone, by tak
ing possession of Arecibo, will be able
to prevent the transportation of fur
ther supplies to San Juan and the pre
dicament of the Spanish troops there
will be thus made most desperate.
Three vessels of the American fleet
operating about Porto Rico have been
ordered away. They are the Massachu
setts, Columbia and New York. The
Columbia, which grounded on a reef
near Guanica, has been taken off in
deep water again and all three vessels
have gone to Guantanamo.

BLOCKADE RUNNER
TAKEN INTO PORT
Clever Capture of a Spanish Schooner
by the Little Steam Launch.
of the Gunboat
Bancroft.
KET WEST, Aug. 3. â The Nor
wegian steamer Franklin of about 500
tons, bound from Vera Cruz with a
cargo of food supplies, was captured by
the converted yacht Siren on Monday
off Francis Key, near Calbarien, and
was brought here to-day by a prize
crew under command of Lieutenant
Littlefield. She had already landed a
portion of her cargo when caught.
The converted yacht Eagle, under
command of Lieutenant Southerland,
which arrived from the blockade off the
Isle of Pines on the south coast of Cuba
this afternoon, brings an interesting
story regarding the capture of a email
Spanish schooner by the crew of the
Bancroft. The Bancroft's steam launch,
unaided, effected the capture in Sigua
nea Bay, Isle of Pines, while the Ban
croft and the Eagle were reconnoitering
at Maugh Point, farther up the catst.
The little schooner, in charge of the
boatswain's mate and one seaman, was
sent to an anchorage near the place
where the steamer Santo Domingo was
sunk some time ago while attempting
to run the blockade.
Two hours later upon the arrival of
the Eagle and the Bancroft at their
anchorage the little prize was observed
close up to the wreck of the Santo Do
mingo with the Spanish flag flying at
her masthead. On the schooner's deck
several men could be seen, indicating
that the vessel had been recaptured by
the Spaniards.
Captain Clover immediately sent the
Maple to the rescue. On arriving along
side he ascertained that the mate in
charge of the prize had, by a clever
ruse, captured two more boats with
their crews, which were stripping the
Santo Domingo. Seeing wreckers at
work the mate had hoisted the Spanish I
flag, and, sailing close enough to the
Spaniards to get them under range of
his rifles he demanded and received the
surrender of the two boats and their
six occupants.
SPAIN'S REPLY
IN THE HANDS
OF McKJNLEY
Agrees to the General Terms as Laid
Down in the Note prom the
President.
SeeKs to Obtain Concessions on Certain Points,
but porn)al AcceptaQce Is Not Far
Distant.
NEW YORK, August 3.â The Washington correspondent of the Herald sends the
following: An early peace is assured. Spain's reply, formally presented to the President
late this afternoon by Embassador Cambon, leaves no further room for doubt that she
will accept the general terms as to the conditions for a cessation of hostilities laid down in
the President's note. Formal acceptance is all that is lacking. And while Embassador
Cambon is still arguing for additional concessions and asking for the details of the gen
eral terms proposed by the President his interview with Mr. McKinley to-day left no fur
ther room for doubt that a treaty of peace at an early date would be the result of the
pending negotiations.
It is probable several notes will be exchanged with Madrid before the treaty of peace
is finally confirmed. While Spain's latest communication leaves no room for doubt that
it is her intention to accept the general propositions laid down by the President she is,
through Embassador Cambon, arguing several important points.
For instance, the Sagasta Ministry wants to know what is to be done to prevent
the insurgents carrying on the war and committing outrages in other parts of the Phil
ippines while the United States occupies Manila and the immediate surrounding territory.
Spain wants more details as to the evacuation of Cuba. She insists that her soldiers
should leave both Cuba and Porto Rico with all the honors of war, and that the evacu
ation is not to be considered in the light of a surrender. The question of transportation
of her troops â who is to pay the bills and which nation is to preserve order in the island
pending evacuationâ are also matters touched upon in Spain's note.
There are no material changes, however, in the general terms proposed by Presi
dent McKinley, and as Spain has, clearly indicated a willingness to accept these terms
the authorities confidently expect to-night there will be an official announcement of a
cessation of hostilities before the end of the week.

ernment to the President's note, upon

CAMARA'S SQUADRON IS
PRACTICALLY USELESS
None of His Ships Fit to Go Into
Actionâ Should Peace Fail They
Will Seek Refuge.
NEW YORK, Aug. 3.â A dispatch to
the Tribune from Gibraltar says:
It is now an open secret that not a
single fighting ship of Admiral Cama
ra's squadron is in fit condition to g-o
into action. The Pelayo's boilers have
given out, the firing gear and turrets
of the Carlos V are unmanageable, the
Audaz is completely used up by the re
cent excursion to Suez, and the arma
ments of the auxiliary cruisers are
lamentably defective.
In case of any hitch in the peace ne
gotiations the Pelayo and Carlos V
would under no circumstances under
just as It was expected â either a coun
ter-proposition or a request for a fuller
statement in detail upon some of the
heads of the President's note.
Up to the middle of the afternoon it
was stated by all parties concerned
that the Spanish answer had not been
received and that the only formal note
that has come to hand was one from
the Madrid Cabinet to M. Cambon, ask
ing for information upon some points
that were not clear to the Spanish Min
isters. However, just after 3 o'clock the
Secretary of the French Embassy, M.
Thiebaut, called at the State Depart
ment and arranged with Secretary
Day for a meeting betweeen the Presi
dent and the Embassador as soon as
possible. The meeting was set for 3:45
o'clock, and Secretary Day was at the
take an engagement here or in the
open sea, but would take refuge be
hind the formidable forts of Cartagena.
The auxiliary cruiser Alfonso XIII
and the Peiayo have sailed from Cadiz
for the Canaries.
Admiral Camara, with his flagship,
the Peiayo, the Carlos V, the Rapido.
the Patriot, the Audaz. the Geraldo and
the Buenos Ayres, still remain.
The garrison at Cadiz now consists
of about 26,000 men. On Friday and
Saturday additional torpedo defenses
and mines were laid in the entrance
to Cadiz harbor. The lighthouses re
main extinguished. Three guns of 24
centimeter caliber have been placed in
position at San Felipe, but their sup
ply of ammunition is limited.
HUNDREDS TURN BACK
AT THE YUKON'S MOUTH
Intended Treasure-Seekers Sell Their
Outfits and Attempt to Reach
the States.
VICTORIA, B. C, Aug. 3.â The
steamer Manuense arrived from St.
Michael, Alaska, to-day with 260 pas
sengers and between $150,000 and $200,
000 in gold dust. Of the list of pas
sengers only thirty-four were miners
who had spent as much as one winter
in the mines, the remainder being
those" who had gone there this spring
and concluded not to remain. The pas
sengers all agreed in the statement that
hundreds of men are arriving in St.
Michael daily from Dawson and vicin
ity seeking passage to the States, and
that there are upward of 1000 men in
that part who have lately come from
coast ports and are trying to get up
the river. Many are turning back af
ter learning the conditions at Dawson
and up river points.
A larger part of the Manuense"s pas
, sengers are returning to their homes in
California, Washington and Dakota.
The river steamer Monarch is report
ed aground about 250 miles from Daw
son.
St. Michael, harbor and the island pre
sent a lively appearance. A forest of
masts covers the bay and the seashore
is lined with people. Boats come in
every day or two from up the river
with scores and hundreds of dis
appointed gold seekers whose stories of
ill-luck and hardships are vividly told

PROMOTIONS ASSURED
HEROES OF THE NAVY
; Board to Be Created to Investigate
and Report the Names of the
Deserving.
NEW YORK. Aug. 3.â A Washing
ton special to the Herald says:
Full recognition of the meritorious gal
lant conduct displayed by American offi
cers and men during the existing war
Is given by President McKinley. That
there may be no just cause for con* lalnt
that the Navy Department overlooked
any meritorious cases Secretary Long
proposes to appoint a board of ranking
navy officers which shall carefully inves
tigate numerous cases brought to its at
tention and report tho cases of the men
to be advanced for heroic service. The
instructions under which the board, will
act will be carefully drawn. It Is not
intended that it shall pass upon the acts
of officers of flag rank or captains. The
President and Secretary Long feel they
can determine for themselves whicn of
these officers are entitled to recognition.
From the present indications the Presi
dent will promote Commodore Schley and
Acting Rear Admiral Sampson in the
order named anu the captains of ships
which participated in the battle with Cer
vera's fleet.
The board will be required to Investi
gate the conduct of officers of lesser rank
who may have participated in the battle
of July 3, that in Manila Bay or any of
the numerous engagements which nave
taken place along the Cuban coast.2

â  surrounding territory. This involves an
immediate surrender of the Spanish
â  forces, riot to Aguinaldo, but to General
AT^rritt, and there is now apprehension
that the insurgents will resent this bit
terly and that It will be necessary to
' expel them forcibly from the territory
described. \

ters that pending the decision of the
peace commissioners as to the future of
the Philippines, the United States will
be morally bound to maintain the statu
quo in the island at large. In other
- words, having deprived Spain of the
; means of resisting the onslaughts of
the insurgents, it has been urged that
the United States would be bound to
prevent the latter continuing their
â¢warfare.
There is a lack of information in
[Washington on many important points
as to the conditions in the Philippines.

000.000. If the revolutionary movement

HARVEST FOR
CANNERYMEN
Salmon Swarming in
Lower Puget Sound.
ENORMOUS RUN IN PROGRESS
LATE IN THE SEASON, STILL
GODSEND.
Great Rejoicing Among the Packers,
Who Were Bitterly Disap
pointed Over the July
Hun.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Aug. 3. â An enormous sal
mon run is in progress on Lower Puget
Sound, causing rejoicing among can
nerym'eh at Blame, Fairhaven and An
acortes. July's smail run was a bitter
di.^Hi'peinUnent to cannery operators,
who began to fear they would have
none to pack. Last Tuesday, however,
ttie situation changed and fish have
been appearing In larger numbers
steadily since. The present run is of
sockeyes, to be followed later by silver
eides,and Chinooks.
Ma-uag.-r Morgan of the Pacific
Northwest Packing Company estimates
this year's pack at SO per cent of last
year's. Considering the lateness of the
'season cannerymen will be well satis
tied with the result. Last year's run
was phenomenal, it being a "fourth"
year, and every fourth year salmon al
ivays appear in greater numbers. Prices
are expected to be good, because of aji
.expected short pack on the Columbia
Paver.
&s#&>' Fish Commissioner Little an
nounces that streams running into the
Columbia River in Central Washington
are becoming barren of salmon. For
merly millions of flsh spawned there
and these streams were the favorite
fishing grounds of the Indian tribes.
Now but few fish get above the numer
ous fish wheels in the lower Columbia,
showing that they are being extermi
nated. To offset this tendency Wash
ington has several big salmon hatch

PHILADELPHIA. Aug. 3.-The exces
sive heat of the last three weeks or morÂ»

GIANT WHISKY COMBINE.
It Will Control Absolutely All the
Business of the Country in Its
Particular Line.
CINCINNATI, Aug. 3.-The United
States Spirit Association, the combination
which proposes to control absolutely the
whisky business of the country, was
formed here to-day, when the following
executive committee was elected: W N
Hobart. Cincinnati, chairman; J. Walter
Freinberg, Cincinnati, secretary; F. T.
Corning, Peorla, 111., treasurer; P. J.
Hennessey. Chicago; Samuel Woolner.
New York; Frank Curtles. New York-
Charles A. Webb, New York.
This committee in vested with the en
tire control of the affairs of the combine.
The meetings here yesterday and to-day
were attended by distillers from all parts
of the country. The Far West was rep
resented by William Wolf & Co., San
Francisco.

REPORT OF GENERAL KENT
BATTALION PANIC-STRICKEN AT
.â¢; ,s:, s : SANTIAGO. ,\' "-'
.
Staff Officers Formed a Cordon Be
' iilnd the Men and Thus Pre
4 â â â  vented a Disastrous
V-;>f '"â¢''-.: -Bout. --. ...-'â â 
Copyrighted, 189S, by the Associated Press.
HEADQUARTERS OF THE FIRST
DIVISION OF THE FIFTH ARMY
CORPS, NEAR SANTIAGO DE CUBA,
July 22 (Correspondence 1 of the Asso
ciated Press).â Major General Shatter
having authorized the publication of
the official report of Brigadier General
J. Ford Kent of the battle of July 1, 2
and 3, a certified copy of the report is
now furnished to .the Associated. Press.
' In his report, which is dated July 7,
General Kent, after detailing the move
ments which brought on the battle of
July 1, gives the first official statement
covering certain incidents of that en
gagement. 3 *
. During the advance General Kent re
ports the division suffered .heavy losses,
caused by the balloon near by attract
ing the enemy's fire and disclosing the
position of the division. Describing the
lighting in the vicinity of the main ford
of the. San Juan River the report says:
â  The enemy's infantry lire, steadily in
creasing in Intensity, now came from all
directions, not only from the front and the
dense tropical' thickets on our flanKÃ, but
from sharpshooters thickly posted in trees
in our rear, and from shrapnel, apparent
ly aimed at the balloon.
Lieutenant Colonel Derby' of Shatter's
staff met me about this time and inform
ed me that a trail or narrow way had
been discovered from the balloon a short
distance leading to the left of the ford,
lower down the stream. I hastened to the
forks made by this road and soon after '
the Seventy-first New York Regiment of
Hawkins' brigade came up. I turned
them into the by path indicated by Lieu
tenant Colonel Derby, leading to the
lower ford, sending word to General Haw
kins of this movement. This would have,
speedily delivered them into their proper
place on the left of their brigade, but un
der the galling- fire of the enemy the
leading battalion of this regiment was
thrown into confusion and recoiled in dis
order on the troops in the rear. " '.'.'.; :
At this critical moment the officers of
my staff practically formed a cordon be
hind the panic-stricken 'men ami urged
them : to again go forward. I finally order- j
ed them to lie down in the thicket and ;
clear' the way for others, ' of their own
regiment,' who were coming up behind.
This many of them did, and the Second
and. Third battalions â¢ -came forward in
better order and moved along the â¢ road
toward the ford. â '*:":
One of my staff officers ran back, wav
ing his hat to hurry forward the Third j
Brigade, who, upon approaching . the, I
forks; found the way blocked by men of ;
the Seventy-first New York. There were ;
Other men of this regiment crouching in |
the bushes, many of whom were encour
aged by the advance of the approaching
column to arise and go forward, â  . .. Â
As -already stated,- I 'had received orders
some- time before to keep in â  the rear of
the cavalry division. Their -advance was
much slower, resulting in frequent halts,
presumably to drop their blanket rolls
and due to the natural delay in, fording
a stream. .
â¢These delays, under a galling fire, grew
exceedingly irksome, and I therefore
pushed the head of my division as quick
ly as I 'could toward the river in column
or.files of twos paralleled on the narrow,
way by the cavalry. This quickened the
following movement and enabled me to"
get into position as speedily as possible
for the attack. â¢" Owing to the congested
condition of the road the progress" of the
narrow column was, however, painfully
slow!- . .:^c
â¢ I again sent a staff officer at a gallop
to urge forward the troops' in the rear.
The head of Wyckoff's brigade reached
the forks at 12:20 p. m. and hurried on
the left, stepping over prostrated forms
of men of the Seventy-first. This heroic j
brigade, consisting of the Thirteenth,
Ninth and- Twenty-fourth. United States
Infantry, speedily, crossed the stream and
were quickly deployed to the left of the
lower ford. While personally superintend
ing this movement. Colonel Wyckoff was
killed, the command of the brigade then
devolving upon Lieutenant Colonel
Worth, Thirteenth Infantry, who imme
diately fell, severely wounded, and then
upon Lieutenant Colonel Liscum, Twen
ty-fourth Infantry, who, five 'minutes
later, also fell under the withering fire of
the enemy; The command of the brigade
then devolved- upon Lieutenant Colonel
T. P. Ewers, Ninth Infantry. V;\
Meanwhile I had again sent a staff of
ficer to hurry forward the Second Brig
ade, which was bringing up the rear.
The Tenth and Second Infantry, soon ar
riving at the forks; were deflected to
the left to follow the Third ! Brigade,
while the Twenty-first was directed along
the main road to support Hawkins.
Crossing the lower ford a few minutes
later, the Tenth and Second moved for
ward in column in good order toward the
green knoll already referred to as my
objective on the left. Approaching the
knoll the regiments deployed/passed over
the knoll and descended the high ridge
beyond, driving back the enemy in the
direction of his trenches. I observed this
move from the fort on San Juan hill.
Colonel E. P. Pearson, Tenth Infantry,
commanding the Second Brigade, and of
ficers and troops under his command, de
serve great credit for the soldierly man
ner in which this movement was S exe
cuted. I earnestly recommend Colonel
Pearson for promotion.
Prior to this advance of the Second Bri
gade, the Third, connecting with Haw
kins" gallant troops on the right, moved
toward Fort San Juan, sweeping through
a zone of most destructive fire," scaling
the : steep and difficult hill and assisting
in capturing the enemy's strong position.
Fort San Juan, at 1:30 p. m. This crest
was about 125 feet above the general
level and was defended by deep trenches
and a loopholed brick fort, surrounded by
barbed wire. â  .
The greatest credit is due to the offi
cers of my command, whether company,
battalion, regiment or brigade com
manders, who so admirably directed the
formation of their troops, unavoidably
intermixed In the dense thicket, and
made the desperate rush for the distant
and strongly defended crest. .
General Kent praises the members
of his staff and urges that they should
at least be brevetted for gallantry un
der fire, and he particularly praises
the conduct of T. J. Kirkpatrick, as
sistant surgeon, on duty with the
Twenty-fourth Infantry, and First
Lieutenant J. D. Miley, Fifth Artillery,
aid to General Shatter. The conduct of
Brigadier General H. S. Hawkins is
also highly commended.
SOLANO'S GREATEST FIRE.
VACAVILLE. Aug. 3.â A fierce fire has
been raging in this vicinity s'nee July 27.
It started northwest of town at the head
of Miller's Canyon, within two miles of
Pirtha Creek, and has been burning ever
since. Between 30.000 and 40,000 acres have
been burned over and eight houses, and
as many barns and about 400 cords of
wood destroyed, to say nothing of the loss
of fences. The intense heat caused by the
flames has ruined half the grape crop and
damaged fruit trees In the valley to the
extent of at least $TiOOO. Hundreds of wild
animals of all kinds have been driven out
of the forests into the vicinity of farm
houses and cultivated fields. It Is cer
tainly the most disastrous fire that ever
occurred in Solano County.

WILL BE BURIED
IN A CATHEDRAL
BUILT BY NATURE
The Oaks and Beeches Bismarck
Loved So Well to Canopy
His Tomb.
Special cable to The Call and the New Tork
Herald. Copyrighted, 1888, by JaraeÂ« Gor
don Bennett.
FRIEDRICHSRUHE, Aug. 3.â The
publication of Bismarck's memoirs Is
most unlikely for. years to oome, as
this has been left entirely In the hands
of Prince Herbert, and gratification of
public curiosity is about the last thing
in the world to influence him. Lothar
Brecher told me before he died that
Bismarck's memoirs consisted then of
two bulky volumes, already complete
in themselves, and that one would be
published during the Prince's lifetime
and another after his death. These
dispositions were as Indicated above
and altered since Lothar Brecher died.
Dr. Schweninger quitted Friedrlchs
ruhe at midnight. Before he left we
strolled up the hill together, on which
the mausoleum will be built, and sat
down on a rude -wooden bench on
which Bismarck rested, where he used
to lean on his stick and gaze over the
valley, and where, sitting -with Dr.
Schweninger, the idea struck him that
he would like to be buried at that par
ticular spot.
"Yes," the doctor mused, "here he
will have a cathedral all to himself,
arched over by the oaks and beeches
ho loved so well."
Dr. Schweninger tells me that his
life's work was over when Bismarck
died. This was his sacred task,
guarding that precious life. Bis
marck's bright humor occasionally
shone forth in all its radiance until the
last, but his animal spirits visibly de
clined during the final few montb.3.
The complete isolation in which he
lived added materially to depress him.
He used to long for Schweninger to
come. "I am glad you have come. I
turn to others, but they do not under
stand me; what I say is to them like
water running down the back of a peli
can," he would declare sadly.
One of the saddest features connect
ed with the last few months of his lite
was that his favorite hounds, his fond
est companions, died before him. No
one remains to-day to whine and sor
row beside his bier.

voted son of the Fatherland and as the
most faithful servant of his Emperor

Yesterday's service at Frledrichsruhe
will be the only obsequies until the
mausoleum on the site of the famous
slag group near the Schloss is com
pleted. The national obsequies have
been postponed until that time.
BERLIN, Aug. 3.â The influx of tele
grams, letters and wreaths at Frled
richsruhe is unceasing. Already the
Schloss overflows with wreaths and
those arriving later must now perforce
be placed in the garden.
When Prince Herbert declined a sec
ond time the Emperor's proffer of a
grave in the Berlin Cathedral his Maj
esty indicated a plan for a national
monument. It is understood he pro
poses to place in the cathedral an im
mense marble sarcophagus, with the
figure of Bismarck in the uniform of a
cuirrassier.
Rather an unseemly wrangle has
arisen in the press concerning the ex
act causes and circumstances of the
Prince's death, and the fact that no
autopsy was held makes it impossible
to silence scandal-loving tongues.
Professor Virchow. interviewed on the
subject, declined to express an opinion
on the vague information available, but
said he was disposed to think that the
death of the Prince was painless.

ern Squadron.

HIS FLAGSHIP.
Retention of Several Armor Clads in

Significant by Naval
Officers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Aug. 3.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent telegraphs:
Preparatory to Its departure on its mis
sion abroad the Eastern squadron Is
being reorganized by Secretary Long.
The cruiser Newark, original flagship
of the squadron, will be retained 'n
north Atlantic waters and will proba
bly ht-come the flagship of Commcdore
Pchley The Massachusetts, Ne.var,c
and Columbia are on their way to Gu
antanamo from Porto Rico. Upon their
ar-lval the Columbia will be given or
ders to assume such duty as may be
designated by Rear Admiral Sampson,
tie Massachusetts will be attached to
Commodore Watson's command and UiQ
Newark will, it Is said to-night, rece.vc
the Hag of Commodore Schley. lnia
ivonns that Commodore Schley will rs
nmn at home uhile the Brooklyn will
be attached to Commodore Watsons
command.
It is expected that it will be necessary
to make a few repairs to the Massachu
setts and when these are completeu the
Eastern squadron will be ready to start
ab-< ad. The retention of several armor
clads In American waters is conslde.-i
nest significant by naval officers with
whom I talked to-day, especially wh^ii
tnken :n conjunction with the repeated
statements of Secretary Long and other
officials that the original orders to the
fleet had not been modified. If the ad
ministration carries out its plan of dis
patching strong reinforcements to the
Philippines It will be a notice to all
Europe that the United States does not
propose to let any foreign complications
grow out of the final settlement in re
gard to the Islands.

PRECINCT REGISTRATION.
Office of the Registrar of Voters of the
City and 00-unty of San Francisco.
Notice is hereby given that the Boards
of Precinct Registration for the general
election to be held on Tuesday, the Bth
day of November, A. D. IS9B. will meet
in their respective precincts for the regis-
tration of voters and such other purposes
as are prescribed by law, on Wednes-
day, August 10, 1898, from 9 o'clock a: m.
until 10 o'clock p. m. daily for five (5)
days, until and including Monday, August
15, IS9B, on which day all registration
closes.
All electors who have not been regis-
tered for said general election are hereby
Invited to present themselves for regis-
tration.
The Boards of Precinct Registration will
sit as above for the purpose of register-
ing all legal and qualified voters residing
In their precincts who have not regist-
ered at the office of the Registrar of
Voters since June 1, 1898.
Any person who has not registered
since June 1, IS9B. and fails to register
at the Boards of Precinct Registration
of the precinct in which he resides will
be debarred the privilege of voting at
such election.
In accordance with section 21 of the
"Act to Regulate the Registration of
Voters," etc., the following places have
been secured for the sessions . of the
Boards of Precinct Registration:
REGISTRATION PLACES.

WELLS-FARGO'S
PETTY SCHEMING
ONCE MORE FAILS
Each Separate Newspaper Bun
dle Must Carry a War Tax
Stamp.
WASHINGTON, 'Aug. 3.â "Wells, FargtJ & Co. are not to have things
their own way. No sooner was the war tax Imposed than they sought
to evade Its provisions. After an obstinate . strusrgle they have been
compelled to yield on one or two points and bear their Just share of
the war tax. .
Now comes a ruling to-tiny from Commissioner of Internal Rev
enue Scott that forces the express company once more to come to
time. It has been the contention of Wells, Fargo & Co. that where
a newspaper ships bundles of papers to its agents throughout Cali
fornia and other coast States but one bill of lading shall be issued for
each wagon load, the company refusing to issue a bill of lading for
each package. The ruling Is made on the appeal of the intelligencer
Publishing Company of Wheeling, W. Va. The Commissioner de*.
cides with regard to newspapers that the general bundle must have
one destination and packages cannot be taken therefrom and deliv
ered at intermediate points. â¢ â¢
The Intent of the art is ruled to be that each bundle, having a dif
ferent destination, should have a different bill of lading, and. conse
quently, its appropriate stamp. It Is, therefore, advised that each
package for each place should have Its bill of lading and its one cent
stamp.

GOLD HUNTERS
COMING HOME
Flocking in Droves From
Copper River.
SAME OLD STORY OF M 0 DUST
FIVE OAKLANDERS ARRIVE AT
JUNEAU.
Tell of the Straggling Mass of Hu
manity on the Valdez Glacier
and the Perils They Are
Subjected To.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
.JUNEAU, Alaska, July 28 (by Steam
ship Oregon to. Seattle, Aug. 3).â The
steamship Excelsior came in this even
ing and 130 more Copper River kickers
walked ashore here. The Oregon came
in s"nÂ«n after, bound south, and nearly
all of them, taking advantage of. a
special rate, left for the Sound on her.
Chasers of. the golden fleece are now
flocking back over the glacier from the
Copper River Valley in droves, taking
advantage of the remaining short sea
son only during which it is possible to
go either backward or forward.
W. Luckman of Oakland, Cal., Bays
there were not less than 1000 men on
the glacier and traveling for it when
he came out seven days ago. He says
that by this time there must be between
300 and 400 . at Valdez waiting for a
steamer. The price of grub has fallen
and this fact makes it more difficult,
in fact almost Impossible, for men who
have no money to- sell their outfits for
money to pay their passage home.
Flour, for instance, en the other side
of the glacier is $1 per sack and coffee
5 cent per pound. All other passengers
have the same story of no gold and
hard luck to tell, and all agree that
they might as well attempt to swim up
Niagara Falls as to get any kind of a
boat up the. Copper River, which is a
roaring torrent.
Two soldiers built a bridge across a
small stream. The bridge was washed
away and the soldiers remained an
the farther side for three days and
nearly starved to death because it was
not possible . to wade the stream or
otherwise get assistance to them. Cap
tain Abercrombie and his men are now
attempting to make a trail that will
make the exodus easier.
Others from Oakland besides Luck
man are B. Lueneburg, H. li. Schnoor.
F Coons and Al Wiseman. They were
among the party of forty-four which
left Oakland on the schooner Laulnfa,
September 22, 1897, for Copper River.
They are returning with but little
more than the clothes on their backs,
with their steam launch and whale
boat left in the Conner River section
they do not know where. Most of
this party of forty-four are still up in
the Copper River country, though a
few managed to return home earlier in
the season. Luckman said he did not
know Where, they all were, as they
were soon divided, scattered and lost
to each other.
The steamer Wolcott has DPen pulled
off the route and put on cannery busi
ness, and the Excelsior has now almost
a monopoly of the return travel from
Valdez. HAL HOFFMAN.
WHALER ALEXANDER
AMONG THE MISSING
ST. MICHAEL, Alaska, July 21 (via
Victoria, Aug. 3).â Grave fears are en
tertained for the safety of the steam
whaler Alexander of San Francisco.
The whalers Bayliss and Karluk from
north of Bering Straits, under date of
June 27, send a dispatch that the Alex
ander was last sighted off Fox Island
in the Aleutian group in April. She
was then in what might become a dan
gerous position. The fleet proceeded
north and did not become alarmed un
til late in June, when the Alexander
failed to show up. All other whalers
that were in the vicinity of Fox Island
have reached Bering Straits and re
ported.

DISSENSION
STIRS THE
DISSENTERS
Another Discord in the
Democratic Ran â¢
M'NAB'S FATAL MISTAKE
camg jgaMjuÃ <* ~~ . â¢ . â¢
NAMED A FEW HONEST MEN" FOR
HIS COMMITTEE,.
Francis J. Heney Gives His Reasons
for Opposing the Programme
â¢ of ' the ""Whispering
' ';.- Boss." :
Dissension has entered the ranks of the
dissenters and another note of discord is
swelling the medley of discordant sounds
that are coming from the camp of the lo
cal Democracy.
It appears "Whispering" Gavin McNab
WILL HE STOP HOWLING WHEN HE GETS IT?
One Bottle Does Not Satisfy the Healthy Appe
tite of This Kid.
has blundered, arid that In selecting the
men who now comprise the hand-made
City and County Committee of the Demo
cratic party of San Francisco he made
the fatal mistake of naming a fewâ a very
fewâ good Democrats. He did not mean
to do it,- but even reformers sometimes
blander. It was at the meeting of the
committee Tuesday night that the whis
pering boss was brought to a full realiza
tion of his mistake, through the open
and earnest protest of a few members of
the committee against the proposition to
appoint delegates to the State convention
and thus prevent 80,000 voters of this city
from having a voice In the selection of the
men who are to represent them in a body
that Mill place In nomination all of the
candidates for State offices. Unfortu
nately for the aforesaid voters, the pro
tent was without avail, for the reason
that the honest men were In a hopeless
minority.
The man who led the revolt against the
programme of the whispering boss was
Francis J. Heney. He was supported by
seven members of the committee, all of
whom declared against the proposition of
appointing delegates instead of submit
ting the mater to the voters of the party
through the medium of a primary elec
tion. HÂ« now proposes to fight the pro
grammers on their own ground, for the
purpose oÂ£ securing a primary election
for the selection of delegates to the muni
cipal convention. His views on the sub
1 Ject are Buch as will prove interesting to
the followers of the whispering boss. In
discussing the subject yesterday he said:
"In making my 'light before the commit
tee of one hundred last Tuesday night I
was earnest and sincere. It Is true that
I failed to secure the holding or prima
ries for the selection of delegates to the
State convention, and as the time inter
vening between now and the time for the
holding of the said convention is short
it would be useleÃS to make any further
attempt to hold such primaries.
"The municipal election, however, is
another matter, and there is ample time
to hold primaries, to purge the rolls and
in every way by honest and fair means to
elect delegates who are the choice of the
people. I will earnestly fight for a pri
mary to elect delegates to a municipal
convention, and have formulated a plan
which I will present to the commuteÂ© at
its next meeting. The proposition is so |
fair and it has all of the elements .neces
sary to secure for the people an honest
expression of their will that the commit
tee will not dare to refuse to adopt It.
"The hue and cry made against the
holding of primaries on the ground that
they are not fair is foundeasolely on the
proposition that, the officers conducting
the primary elections have heretofore al
ways been corrupt and have permitted
fraudulent voting. Of course the election
officers cannot be expected to be any purer
or any more earnest and sincere In tneir
desire to conduct a fair election than are
those, from whom . they receive their au
thority to act; .
"If the election officers are appointed
by Buckley or are named by him directly
or indirectly no honest man will expect
that an honest expression of the will of
the Democratic party could be secured
through such a medium. I propose, how
ever that the election officers In each
and 'every club throughout tne city in
which a primary election is held shall t>e
appointed by John P. Dunn, the chaiwnan
of the committee of one hundred, and
that he shall make these appointments
by and with the advice of the members
of said committee who reside in the re
spective Assembly districts in which the
clubs are located. If the committee of
one hundred desires to get a true and hon
est expression of opinion of the will of
the Democratic party in this city as to
who shall compose the delegation to the
convention to nominate our city officers,
that committee can form Democratic
clubs In- each Assembly District and can
itself appoint a man to. act as president
of each of said clubs, he â to have sole
Dower to supervise and control the
enrollment of the Democratic voters in
hIR district.
"All the rolls can be closed a month
before the time fixed for holding the elec
tion of delegates and the rolls can be
thoroughly purged by the committee dirr
lng that month. On the night of the club
election the committee can have Its own
officers In charge of the ballot box and
there can be men selected from the resi
dents who are best acquainted with tho
members of the ctub.
"In any club where It might be ex-
pected that force would be attempted to
intimidate honest men or prevent them
from voting arrangements could be made
to have some of the police present to pre
serve order and protect the voter from
any hoodlum element that might try to
make trouble. â 
"The committee would of Itself be the
returning board and pass upon the elec
tion of all delegates and determine If a
certificate should be issued or refused.
In every case where the election officers
reported that they had failed to secure
an honest vote on account of fraud or
force said committee could refuse to is
sue a certificate of election to any dole
gate claiming one if it found such state*
ments on the part of the officers to be
true. The committee could then proceed
to elect a delegate to represent such dis
trict or could declare any of the candi
dates for election in such districts whom
it found to be defeated by such fraud or
force to be the duly elected delegate.
"If a fair expression of the will of the
masses of the voters of the Democratic
party of this city cannot be secured In
this manner it must be because the com
mittee of one hundred, of which I have
the honor to be a member, is Incapable
of governing the party through any of
the methods recognized as proper tor de
termining the will of the people under a
popular form of government.

Democrats Are Preparing for a
Parade and a Reception at
Metropolitan Temple.
About fifty members attended the regu
lar meeting of the Central Democratic
Club last evening at Pythian Castle.
President Holcomb presided, and at the
opening of the meeting read a telegram
from the club's idol. "Little Giant" Ma
guire, who is In Chicago, to the effect
that he will arrive in this city Monday
or Tuesday evening next. To-day Ma
guire will "wire the exact time of his ar
rival. Arrangements are about com
pleted for a parade and reception to bo
given the Populist gubernatorial candi
date. The marching members of the
Iroquol* and other Democratic clubs will
meet him at the ferry and escort him to
Metropolitan Temple, where the oral
pyrotechnics of the occasion will be dis
charged. By a unanimous vote Walter P.
Stradley, ex-Commissioner of Im
migration, was selected to preside
at the reception. The Sailors
Union sent a letter of regret
that the organization could not partici
pate as a body in the welcome, although
every man before t ne mast had the great
est admiration for lr. Maguire.
The committee having charge of the
reception reported that it had been de
cided to reserve the lower portion of
Metropolitan Temple for the Democrats
who participated in the parade. The gal
lery will be open to the public. T. Carl
Spelling of the joint committee to prepare
a new primary law to be submitted to the
next Legislature reported progress, and
stated that the meeting of the committee
will be held Friday evening.
After routine business had been dis
posed of, several embryo orators eulo
gized Maguire. Attorney Cabanlss was
called en for a speech, and during his re
marks said a great deal about an impend
ing marriage between John Bull and Miss
Columbia, but he did not refer to the
"engagement" between Miss Democracy
and Papa Populist, which was recently
arranged at Sacramento, through the me
dium of a shotgun in the hands of the
well-wishers of "Baby" Maguire and the
legitimacy of his position in political so
ciety.
HARNEYITES CONTINUE
WAR PREPARATIONS
District Enrollment Now in
Progress and Will Be Com
pleted To- Night.
The Harney faction of the Democratic
party of this city are continuing their
preparations for. war. Enrollment of
voters in the several Assembly districts
began last night and will be concluded
this evening. At the Harney primaries
each voter will be compelled to subscribe
to the following: "I am a Democrat, and
pledge myself to support the nominees of
the Democratic party at the next elec
tion."

GENERAL MERRIAM WILL
REVIEW THE SEVENTH
GREAT PREPARATIONS FOR THB
BIG RECEPTION.
A Bousing Farewell to Be Given tho
Departing California Boys on
Saturday Night.
Major General H. C. Merrlam. Com
mander-in-chief of all the forces now en
camped about the State, has declared his
willingness to inspect the Seventh Cali
fornia Volunteer* on Saturday evening
next on the occasion of their farewell re
ception. That the affair will be a grand
one is already assured. Distinguished
soldiers and civilians have accepted in
vitation* to be present and sooiety ha 3
stamped Ita approval by purchasing re-
Berved >â¢â  fttfl in sections. The tickets for
th. Mr drill w.-re issued yesterday, and
already a large advance sale is recorded.
As the proceeds of the evening's enter
tainment will be devoted to an emergency
fund for the regiment, and as it is com,
poeed almost entirely of California boy 3
the attendance is expected to tax tha
Pavilion to its utmost capacity.
Residents in the Western Addition hava
been greatly shocked lately at the con
stant ringing of their doorbells by men.
attired in military clothes who appeal for
blankets to keep out the damp night air
that they claim pervades their camp. In
many instances their requests have been
granted but in most cases refusals have
been given owing to the published stories
of impostors who have carried on tne
traffic. The continued reports of this un
mllltary visitation has l^d the Red Cross
Society to warn kind-hearted people not
to give the men what they ask for, be
lieving that they are professional beg
gl The Red Cross Society has shc/wn Its
willingness time and again to step into
the breach when the Government Is un
able to furnish the men with what tney
nepd if properly applied for, and it 13
willing to continue its good work.
W P Whittier, the well-known capital
ist, has given a splendid sign of recogni
tion for the work being done by the Red
Cross. Yesterday he sent a carload of
oranges and lemons to the society. The
generous donation was apportioned out
to the various camps, hospitals and bar
racks about the bay and distributed
among the men. â Â»Â»,.Â«,
Every day more ladies apply to Mrs
Theodore E. Smith, manager at 16 Post
street, for employment, and they are im
mediately put to work on the caps, chest
protectors and pajamas for the men at
camp. There is room for more help, and
it is hoped that more will apply.
The State Department received three
letters from nurses on board the trans
ports in which the men say they have
been accorded the best of treatment and
are giving great help to the different sur
geons. Dr. McCarthy has been extreme
ly pleased with the help given him.
Acting Assistant Surgeon G. H. Rich
ardson on hoard the transport Peru
writes Mrs. J. F. Merrill that the Rf-.l
Cross supplies have been of inestimable
value and that the men are deliffhted
with the Red Cross Society.

William S. Wood, the man who robbed

WELLS-FARGO CALLED DOWN.
' ZHJ VER since the imposition of the war tax, and
the effort of the Wells-Fargo people to evade
Iâ * payment of their share of it, this paper has
deemed it a plain duty to call the attention of the
corporation to the fact that the evasion was un
patriotic and illegal. It quoted the statute, cited the
example of other Jess greedy corporations, and strove
to awaken within the express company some com
punciion of conscience, some indication of a desire
to be honest. But al! in vain.
The company claimed the right to make its cus
tomers pay lor the stamp required to be affixed to
bili> of lading, and, to soothe any impulse toward
protest, offered to help the customer cheat the Gov
ernment. For instance, it said that it would accept
as one bundle enough bundles to constitute a. wagon
load, this generosity applying particularly to news
papers. The error was gently and firmly pointed out
to the sinning corporation, but it. manifested no
symptom of reform. â  . . .
Now Commissioner of Internal Revenue Scott has
passed r.pon the exact point at issue, and says
specifically that no such subterfuge is to be permitted.
Of course everybody knew this, but the company was
hoping for delay until the cruel war should be over.
Mr. Scott says there must be a bill of lading for each
bundle, and that a collection of bundles having dif
ferent destinations cannot be construed as one.
It will be recalled that Wells-Fargo had sought to
have the matter kept in abeyance in order that the
decision of the court might' be .handed down, that as
fast as such decision was handed down an appeal had
been taken to the Supreme Court, where there was a
certainty the matter would rest for a term of years.
It seemed to have an influence sufficient to keep Dis
trict Attorney Foote . from taking any action, and
meantime it was escaping its just share of the com
mon burden. From the Scott decision, however,
there is no appeal. Wclls-Fargo has an opportunity
to show how gracefully it can come down from its
untenable position. It has been called down. hard.
The public will be glad. The assurance of a
wealthy company, doing business under the protection
of the Government, acquiring extra dividends by rea
son of the troubles of the country, yet refusing to pay
any part of the expense, was an irritating insult.
That Wells-Fargo will be ashamed of itself is too
much to hope, but. like a burglar in the grip of a
policeman, it may lead a better life.
STANDING BY OUR GUN.
A WICK ED and ribald attempt has been made
to class the cannon of The Call among
nuisances. Of course everybody will recognize
in this effort the jealousy of the baffled . contem
poraries who neither have a gun, a place to put one
nor the enterprise to buy ammunition.
As a matter of fact The Call cannon is a useful
affair, nineteen stories high in the estimation of the
community. When it speaks from its lofty perch the
noise is merely distinct, and in no sense distressing.
It is a signal. At" the boom of the gun people rush
to their front doors, knowing that something is
happening and desirous of seeing it. That faithful
gun has said "Hail" to the coming soldier, arid to the
outward bound "Farewell." As it sends a puff of
smoke into the sky there is no report shaking win
dows and denting the auricular drum. There is
merely sound enough to attract attention. People
have learned the significance of it. They know the
artillery is not turned loose on any. such fool impulse
as may actuate a siren. It means business every time.
It is as true as the ball which falls at noon, melodious
as the peal of an organ, honest as the curfew bell. It
never gives false alarms, raises bogus hopes nor
booms a fake edition. .
Anybody who does not like that cannon, does not
admire its frankness, acknowledge its probity, either
has dyspepsia or is lanie in his patriotism.
Ever since the Examiner erected at Third and
Market streets a building of lath and plaster it has
boasted that other improvements in the vicinity are
due to the presence of its architectural . freak. The
suspicion cannot be avoided that the Examiner occa
sionally has fun with itself.
An Oakland man who killed his w v ife in cold blood
has been convicted of murder in the. second degree,
whereupon a question naturally arises as to What the
jury would be convicted of were it to be placed on
trial. â '. ".' : â¢
If the chap who rejoices in the title "King of the
Dudes" would grasp the conviction that he is. a harm
less but rather tiresome as.s he would have himself
correctly sized up.
People who received bribes from Hooley are busy
explaining that they came as gifts. Yes?
It is a little strange that the Kaiser should, regard
the Bismarck obsequies as any of his funeral.
THE MfIN AND THE OFFICE.
THE political situation in this State furnishes an
object lesson in the wide departure from primi
tive and purer methods.
Men pick themselves out for Governor, for Sen
ator, or for other office, and proceed to make organ
izations, combinations and programmes looking to
personal success. A candidate for Governor insists
upon naming the delegations to nominate him, which
amounts to the nomination of himself by himself.
Candidates for Senator desire the privilege of nom
inating candidates for the Senate and Assembly pre
pledged to vote for their nominator. The matter is
not left to the people for decision. They are not per
mitted to sit in judgment upon the merit of men.
That is passed upon by the men themselves. The
only choice left to the people is on election day, and
that is entirely between two sets of candidates who
have, by these processes, practically nominated them
selves and passed upon their own fitness for office.
It is to be hoped, from the beginning already made,
that the Republican party in California will exhibit
a better method; that it will pay no heed to these
various pretenders and self-seekers, and in a freely
chosen convention freely select candidates who repre
sent, not merely, time-serving and temporary devo
tion to its principles, but abiding belief in them. Let
it be established that in such a convention the office
is to s^ek the man, not the man the office. Let the
right of the people to select freely be restored to
them.
When a candidate has himself selected a delegation
for himself, taking it into a convention to trade for
votes for himself, let it be understood that such dele
gation, being personal property and in no party sense
representative, has no more influence than the can
didate's pocket-comb, penknife or other item of per
sonal property.
When this system of personal politics is destroyed
we ma}' expect a decided improvement in the atmos
phere of official life. The personnel of the public offi
ciary will undergo a change for the better. Public
administration will cease to be a game of personal
advantage and will appear in the interest of the peo
ple, who have instituted government as a means to
the wise and economical transaction of the public
business.
When this freedom is restored to a party to select
its representatives and command them instead of
being commanded by them, merit may hope for
recognition and men will seek preferment by deserv
ing it in the name of their talents and their virtues.
For a long time such qualifications for office have
been subordinated to the capacity for intrigue, and
the faculty of making personal organizations and
cabals strong enough to seize a nomination regardless
of the wishes of the great masses, which make up
parties.
The system too long in vogue has been copied
bodily from that which prevailed in decadent Rome.
The political prizes offered by the rich resources of
the mistress of the world caused politicians to organ
ize bands of retainers, who. opposed force to force
and walked, over the rights of citizens. The choice
â of men for the great offices of the Roman common
wealth came finally to be made by hard knocks in the
streets between bands of bravos. From these phy
sical contests on the plane of a street riot the tran
sition to civil war was natural and easy, and each
change in the great offices finally came to decision in
widespread civil strife. In this way, for three cen
turies. Roman politicians had prepared the way for
Caesar,, and Caesarism remained after him, the per
manent condition of Rome until her vast structure
fell into ruin.
.. From a candidate for Governor naming the dele
gates who are to nominate him. to the enlistment of
partisans armed to right for his elevation is a very
short step indeed. The long step is taken when his
right to practically nominate himself is conceded.
Let it be recorded to the glory of the Republican
party of California that it denied the right to take the
first step and therefore destroyed the possibility that
the second can ever be taken.
THE "HARNEY DEMOCRATS."
THE Maguire organ asks, "What sort of politi
cal species or thing is a Harney Democrat?"
It says it has heard of Jacksonian or Jeffer
sonian Democrats, but it confesses to entire ignor
ance of the peculiar animal known to the press and
people of this city as "Harney Democrats."
As the organ puts this question with considerable
earnestness and is apparently as ignorant as it pro
fesses to be, we do not mind illuminating its intelli
gence. Perhaps we might be in better business â but
let that pass. A conscientious view of one's duty
often compels the performance of disagreeable acts,
and if we were to do only those things which pleased
us we should fall far short of meeting the just expec
tations of the public. For instance, it is sometimes
the awful duty of a man to argue with a polecat, but
we know of no case in which the cat ever either ap
preciated or profited by the self-sacrifice of the per
son who attempted to enlighten it. On the contrary
â but let that pass also.
As we understand it, the Maguire organ grouped
Jeftersonian, Jacksonian and Harney Democrats for
the purpose of making a comparison at once ridic
ulous and striking. The idea of the organ was to
contrast the enduring principles represented by the
Democrats comprised in the first two designations
with the alleged selfish tendencies of those repre
sented by the last named.. Yet the Harney Democrat
pf to-day stands for principles as vital to the republic
as any expressed by the phrases "Jeflfersonian Dem
ocrat" or "Jacksonian Democrat." What are these
principles? . The organ is as familiar with them as
we are. They are the right of the people to a voice
in political party management and the inalienable
privilege of Democrats to protest against bossism
and usurpation â in other words, the principles upon
which political liberty repose.
As the organ well knows, three members of the
Democratic State. Committee have taken forcible
possession of the party machine in this city. They
have appointed a committee to take charge of the
local Demociacy and have authorized it to select
delegates to State and municipal conventions. They
have arranged a cut and dried programme and par
celed out the nominations of both conventions among
their friends. This has all been done by force and
against the will of the people. Demands by Demo
crats for a hearing on the merits of this policy of
outrage and usurpation have been met with sneers
and contumely. The three bosses have laughed in the
faces of those who have asked for open primaries and
from their lofty attitude have looked down with
scorn and contempt upon the struggles of all in the
prrty who have attempted to maintain their political
rights.
The Harney Democrat of to-day represents oppo
sition to this method of conducting popular govern
ment. The Jeffersonian Democrat of 1798 stood for
State's rights and a simple government; the Jack
sonian Democrat of 1832 stood for political equality
and the spoils system; the Harney Democrat of 1898
THE SA^ FRANCISCO CALI,, THURSDAY, AUGUST 4, 1898.
stands for the right of the people to select their rulers
through the machinery of primaries and conventions.
Of the three we regard the Harney Democrat with
the greatest admiration. Jeffersonian and Jacksonian
Democracy have been exploded by time. The former
was disposed of at Appomattox Courthouse, and the
civil service reformers are rapidly getting away with
the latter. But the Harney Democracy will live for
ever. When it expires the republic will expire, for
upon its principles rest the very foundations of politi
cal liberty. If the organ had a spark of patriotism
left in its corrupt carcass it would laud the Harney
Democrats instead of deriding them.
STATEMENTS made by Ernest T. Hooley, the
insolvent promoter of all sorts of enterprises,
have given rise in London to what, taking all
things into consideration, is the gravest iinancial
scandal of the age. The extent of the corrupt oper
ations of Hooley is indeed inferior to that of the pro
moters of the Panama canal swindle in France, and
the number of eminent persons involved in the scan
dal is less; but, on the other hand, the social posi
tion of the accused parties in London is so much
higher than that of the men exposed in Paris that the
British sensation is unquestionably the more startling
of the two.
Hooley's statements, though given in court and
under oath, are of course not to be accepted with un
questioning credulity. The man by his own showing
has been a swindler, a liar, a bribe-giver, and an
all-round confidence man on a large scale. He has
handled millions of money representing the invest
ments of nearly all classes of the British people, and
has come out of his schemes bankrupt in purse, repu
tation and hope. He is now in his despair and malice
fighting back against the society that condemns him,
and seems determined to drag down with him
as many others as he can.
The testimony of such a man given in such a mood
and under such circumstances must always be re
ceived with suspicion. No man accused* by Hooley
ought to be condemned on that testimony alone.
There are some rascals who would just as soon lie
under oath for spite as to lie on the stock exchange
for money, and Hooley may be one of them. That
he is utterly unscrupulous is beyond question. More
over, according to his own evidence he is now try
ing to shift the burden of wrongdoing from his own
shoulders to that of others, and that fact tends to
weaken the force of what he says. It is known that
so long as his operations were successful he took all
the credit to himself, and now that failure has come
upon him the public will not readily believe the blame
must rest upon others.
Briefly stated, Hooley's claim is that his enterprises
failed only because he had to pay such enormous
tribute to blackmailing newspapers and such large
sums to noblemen to induce them to act as directors
of his various companies. The first plea is certainly
not good. Newspapers are not so much given to
blackmailing as certain rascals would like to have
the public believe. Moreover, honest business men
engaged in legitimate enterprises need not fear black
mailers either in England or in this country. If
Hooley paid any London editors for silence it was
only because he was engaged in something that would
not bear investigation, and in that case the so-called
blackmail was only a division of plunder between two
rascals, one of whom -was about as honest as the
other.
The chief, feature of the scandal is that relating to
the nobility. It will be noted that whereas the
Panama scandal involved a considerable number of
French politicians and statesmen, the British case
does not affect that class of men at all. The dig
nitaries accused by Hooley of selling their names and
patronage to worthless stock companies belong with
out exception to the so-called "smart set." That is
to say, thej' are members of that fashionable clique
that leads society and devotes itself, its wealth and its
energies to high teas, fancy dress balls and general
dissipation. With that class of people few persons
will have any sympathy. London will lose nothing
if some of these gaudy birds are stripped of their
peacock plumage. In fact, it will probably be a ben
efit not only for the British public, but for some of
our own people in New York, if a few members of
the London smart set should be sent to penitentiary
and the whole clique permanently retired to ob
scurity.
THE SUBSIDENCE OF POLITICIANS.
ADDRESSING the Republicans of the First
Congressional District of Maine on the occa
sion of his renomination for Congress by
acclamation, Speaker Reed is reported to have said:
"All over the country there is a dearth of political
feeling. I never knew less of it in all the years in
which I have known of such things."
Of course in making the statement the Speaker
used the phrase "political feeling" to signify that
condition of mind brought about among the people
by differences of opinion concerning the greater is
sues before the country. In that sense the statement
is as true of California as of Maine. Political divi
sions have almost ceased to exist among us so far as
national questions are concerned, and all that is left
for this campaign is to elect to office good men who
will uphold the Republican administration at 'Wash
ington and faithfully perform such duties as their
official positions require of them.
All over the Union the same condition of affairs
prevails. Even in Kansas and among the Populists
there is for a time a subsidence of the calamity howls
of agitation and discontent. Reports from that State
announce that all the Populist candidates for Con
gress in the commonwealth have agreed with the
State Central Committee that the war will furnish
the principal topic of discussion by them and their,
supporters in the coming campaign, that the silver
question and everything else is to be crowded into
the background, and that every speaker in the cam
paign will talk patriotism. Â» . .
This extraordinary change in public sentiment and
the political tone of the country from what prevailed
in 1896 may seem strange, and yet it is but the in
evitable reaction from the excessive agitations of that
year. Even had there been no war to distract popu
lar attention from home issues, the people would not
have paid much attention to the Bryanites or to
Bryan himself this year. Nations, like individuals,
occasionally get tired of certain .things, and this na
tion is tired of Populism, free silver, free trade, Alt
geldism and the whole band, gang and combination
of howling reformers.
The issues which now engage the minds of the in
telligent are those which are to grow up out of the
war. These, as the Speaker said, will be trouble
some and will demand for their proper solution the
highest wisdom and truest patriotism of all. On
these issues, moreover, the great mass of the earnest
conservative body of the people will join the
Speaker in his wish â "For my part I hope that all
these problems may be worked out consistently with
our time honored and dearly bought institutions and
with the traditions of our wise forefathers^
ThjE HOOLEY SCANDAL.
FABLES WITHOUT MORALS.
It befell that a certain corporation
possessed sundry rails wherewith the
street was sore encumbered so that the
people made lament, which the Mayor
hearing, pricked up the ears of him,
and to the corporation said: "Remove
the rails and see that ye delay not, lest
I come with my men, lo! an army for
number, and make a three-base hit by
doing it in my capacity of high cocko
lorum."
Now It befell that the corporation had
desired that the rails they be torn up,
but its members said one to another:
"Wist ye not that he/c is a snap, even
a Mayor who can be worked. Behold,
we would that this rusty iron be
yanked, and while we pause that the
cost of the undertaking is gTeat, he
makes promise to essay the task for
us." Then did they laugh merrily, tak
ing many drinks betimes, and the
Mayor did even as he had said. There
upon the corporation hirelings made
much ado to be wroth, but only alone
as to the Mayor was there a single man
in the whole city whom they befooled.
"Why do you walk backward?" asked
a policeman of a distinguished looking
citizen.
"Pardon me," answered the person
addressed. "I was unaware that there
â¢was anything unusual in my method of
locomotion. The truth is that I have
been on the Supreme bench so long that
reversal has become second nature to
me."
As a prentle corrective the policeman
started the pedestrian along, still fac
ing to the rear, and as he speedily re
ve/pfd himself he proceeded on his way
even as other men.
Receiving back his manuscript a con
tributor drew himself up proudly. "I
would have you understand, sir," he
remarked to the editor, "that I am a
poet and that poets are barn, not
made."
"No apology Is necessary," returned
the editor kindly; "I hope T am not so
brutal as to blame you for having been
born."
As the poet ambled thence he was no
ticed to wear an air of unwonted
thoughtfulness.J
War having come upon the land, a
tax was levied, but with great cheer
the people paid it, saying, "This is a
just war and a righteous; surely
each shall bear his part." But a great
corporation which had waxed strong
under the protection and patronage of
the people refused to contribute, and,
being pressed for explanation, frankly
made known the ground of its demur.
"In the first place," explained the good
man at the head of the corporation,
"we don't want to pay, and in the sec
ond place we don't have to pay. See?"
Yet in their blindness the people
could not see; on the contrary, pro
claiming aloud, "This is a bunko
game. Get you a shell and pea outfit
and make no further pretense of de
cency."

the soldiers encamped awaiting a
chance to goto : the front was appall
ing; a lady ventured to speak with a
general about the matter.
"Since you remind me," courteously
replied the general, "the men have been
dyfrng off rather fast. But may I ask
you, madam,' what soldiers are for?"
As the lady had not given this phase
of the subject consideration she -was
obliged to retreat leaving the query
unanswered. .â¢_ â¢
A Spanish warrior reached the gate
of Heaven and was stopped by the
guardian, who queried of him what
was in the package under his arm.
"That," said Blanco, "is my type
writer."
"You will have to leave it outside,"
replied the guardian, firmly.
With haughty mien the Spanish war
rior strode through the gate, but as he
saw an attendant carelessly throw
aside the beloved machine he mur
mured "This is hell," and though they
gave him a harp he refused to play.

Shaf ter was invited by a Garcia, to turn

did not see his way clear to do so.
"Then I understand," said the Garcia,
with evident feeling, "that you think
more of your own flag than you do of
mine. Such in- titude appalls me. Sir,
I shall decline to eat more of your ra
tions unless so be my men can lift a
few after dark."
To emphasize his disgust he stralght
way permitted a Spanish general to
knock the stuffing out of him.

certaining by inquiry the symptoms of

ing.

Once upon a time a Republic had a

MISREPRESENTED BY THE
EXAMINER.
To the Editor of the San x'rancisc.o
Call â Sir: Through the columns of.
your paper permit me to say that the
statement in the Examiner in which I
am quoted as saying that Major Owen
told, me that his nurse's were incom
petent is absolutely false. Major Owen
never made any such statement, nor
did I ever say he did. I know nothing
whatever about the matter, and it was
a gross injustice, to both Major Owen,
and myself that such a report should
appear in any paper unless it be one
"like the Examiner," whose unreliable
ness is sufficient guarantee that the
victims of its carelessness will -not suf
fer censure. Yours respectfully
MARIE L. McDERMOT,
O. R. C. Society, Oakland.

A THRONG of spectators watched j
the loading of horses and mules on
board the ship Tac.oma at â¢Main
street wharf yesterday. , The -work
' â was expeditiously done, the troopers as
. signed the task handling the horses'
â  as though they were mere sacks- of
â¢- freight, Instead of nervous animals. A
rtanoh ,x. well pr.dded, but little 'â¢â¢jr. i
! than a horse and about as. high,- was pro
: vlded. This had doors at both ends.
: These were thrown open and a .quiet"ani
, xnal was led through.' Gerund this came
r another, and when the latter was well In
side both doors were closed.
' "In another moment the box swung high
; â in the air. The action up to this time was.
: so quick that the horse did not rebel.
When he found himself dangling In' the
. air he Invariably Strug] desperately,
' but to no avail. As the box swung back,
the. majority of the horses sank upon
their haunches, as though expecting a
fall. The work was done .without acci-.
dent. The mules were, if anything, more
' tractable than the horses.
.-.The ship has been splendidly outfitted
. .for -the long voyage, under the direction
' of. Lieutenant Cameron. Fourth â  United
' States Cavalry. All that modern science \
-. could suggest for the comfort of the ani
jiials has been applied to their quarters. j
Elect Tic machinery has been installed, by
which the Tacoma will be "lighted I
â  throughout. Fans will be operated when |
the' air of the tropics becomes oppressive,.
.and there will be no lack of fresh water,,
a distilling apparatus, with an enormous
capacity, having been constructed on thai
.vessel. . .'â â . . ;
â Each animal has a padded stall' to it
â Bel-f. The minimum of space Is allowed,
60 that but- little variety of position will '
be possible, but, in a sense, there will be j
â no overcrowding. Xo animal â¢ can en- !
croach' upon his neighbor'^ allotment of
'space. Each stall is provided .'â  with a
jnÂ«Â£tal feed trough, and water faucets
. ha : been applied to piping at frequent
intervals. :. â¢
â¢;. Each stall is provided with a veteri
.nary sling. This is a canvas and -rope
â  affair, by means t>f which sick horses.
. may be suspended. It is- thought that
these, will be necessary after a .severe
storm, and will also permit of the horses'
resting at any time. The mules', more
hardy than their fellows, have- been
. placed on the 'tween decks, while- the
hoTses are on what was originally the
main deck. Above this, however, there

â who go in charge of the horses. The com
fort of the men has been well cared,
for. ' ' â â : â :'

arid. Twenty-fifth Regular Infantry Regi

â¢action. â¢ The- men " endured many hard
ships and were â¢ exposed to a severe; fire
of the enemy.- The Fourth sustained a

fifth was : one offloer killed, .three officers
. wounded, eight enlisted men killed and
â twenty-seven' wounded. The brigade

at 4 o'clock and marched along the road

o'clock we .stopped to rest for five min
utes." : -KÂ»-Â«uming Â°ur "march, we had not
â gone more than a quarter" of a mile when
tho bullets, began falling among us. On
account of the brush, we could not see
whence they came. You can. readily
imagine what a position we were In. Be
1 we had time to think our regiment
had two men killed and ten wounded. As
Boon -as we realized our predicament we
formed a line of battle on the road, and
advanced through tho thick brush to
Borne cleared land, a distance of about a
mile and a half., all the time under a
fierce nr-- from the enemy. When W3
tied the open" we fouml * hf \ ss P aI ?7?i
strongly intrenched on top of a high hill.
from* which they were popplng.it into us.
VfTe fought our way along- the level plaiii,
meeting with great loss. Charging up the
hill we drove the Spanish from their po
sition and compelled them â to retreat,
down the opposite -side and over another
hill to the intrenchments on the plain
below.: -We now took up a. position on the
summit of the. second hill keeping up a
steady .fire at th.c enemy all night and the
â  n<^ln the night of July 2 the Spaniards
came out of their intrenchments and tried
â  to steal up into us. but they 'got it in the

to the Sixteenth and Sixth must be given

flehtlng Our poor regiment was terribly
â  cut up in the battle. Out of 597 men who
.went into the field sixteen were killed
and "IIS wounded, one-third being .mortal
ly" wounded, and seven missing.
"I had a miraculous escape. A piece of
shrapnel penetrated my hat at one s4de.
and came out at the other.- Inflicting a
email scalp woundand hitting a sergeant

Hospital Conditions Improving
The conditions at the division .hospital
are daily improving. While the hospital
is filled to Its utmost capacity the mor
tality is greatly on the decrease. There
have been no deaths reported for the
past two days. The surgeons and nurses i
are working faithfully and since the men
have got into barracks. the surg-oons have '
been able to Cope with cases that would
have been fatal in ten.te.

sergeant major of the regiment, vice Dis
ser, appointed post quartermaster. Ser
geant Brooke's promotion is a deserved
one and he is highly thought of .by his
officers. â¢ .. -

CONDITIONS IN WALL STREET. . â 
-. The circular of Henry Clews, '..under date of
New York. July 30, says: -."A firm. tone pre
.vails in the stock market, but' several factors
Beem to Interfere with its upward movement at j
this time. The big leaders 'are not active, cvi- 1
dently preferring a reaction on which to- accu- j
mulate a new line of stocks to carrying : on the j
advance without interruption.- Until they are I
ready it is useless to' calculate upon booming j
prices; but it is well to observe Influences at 1
work which. In due season, will overcome all ]
artificial barriers, and compel even : the big
leaders to come into line. -We' refer to. the mul
tiplying evidences that the country has already,
entered upon an- era of. exceptional prosperity.
The war is now a factor of secondary impor- ;
.tance. The controlling influences juet now are \
the encouraging conditions in agriculture and
industry. Upon:' these our future prosperity
chiefly rests. .-War -may prove a 'stimulus to
trade, but genuine prosperity . was never built
upon a war basis. It is '-more important, to j
know that the farmers have had one prosperous
.-season an-.! are get mi,- out of debt at -arj aston- â 
ishing rate. Another season of large crops, fair
" prices and good exports Is In prospect. Cotton
Is very low in price, and a large yield is antici
pated; but the cost of production ' has been
greatly reduced, so that growers will riot euf- Â
. fer hardship. The South has also benefited by
â  large fruit crops and 'a decided Improvement In
industrial conditions "there. In New England
the industries, especially textiles. . are not in .
satisfactory shape; but the situation is lmprov-:|
' Ing. In the Central West manufacturing of all '
. sorts Is In active progress. The production of
pig iron during the last six months was 6.000.000 ;
tons; much the largest- half year's production
in our history. Our foreign trade has resulted
In a heavy trade balance in our favor, which
â¢promises a large influx of gold, hot to speak of
the amount likely soon to.be received from- the
'Klondike. The extension of our territory also
foreshadows a more active â¢ foreign trade.
Money Is easy; confidence is rising, arid all the'
â¢chief trade forces seem to be concentrating In '.
the direction of business revival! l.'ruler such
conditions railroad prosperity must be contln- â¢
uous; and it is for such reasons that we have
implicit confidence in higher prices . for stocks. â 
later on." â¢ .

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Aug. 3.â There was no great
outburst of speculative enthusiaFm in the stock
market to-day as a result of the peace news.
The market broadened out materially and was
confidently strong all day, but inquiring -ob
servers feel considerable duubt whether the
peace prospects had much to do with the rise.
Of much more effect Is the generally growing
conviction that the dullness of the local money
mar'--* Is not at all due to sluggish trade con
ditions, but raiher to the abundant supplies of
money at interior points to meet the require
ments of expanding business and the move-
Best thus far of the crnps. Yesterday's gold
lmportÃ resulted In stiffening foreign exchange
ar.d In appreciably easier money rates for time
loans. Exchange experts are not able to figure
any profit on the g-->ld import operation at the
present level of exchange, and the engage
ments must be accepted as antlcipative of
future conditur.s. The general ti-nor of talk
heard in Wall street is all of expected Increase
of trade and industrial expansions. The gen
eral view in the street is unmistakably bullish.
The strength of â¢â¦*-â  market to-day was taken
advantage of to realize profits in some of the
recently manipulated Industrial spsclaltips. but
there was a broad and comprehensive demand
for the standard stocks, the grangers and the
favorite dividend payers leading.
I>ealir.ss in bonds were also active and wf-11
distributed. A feature of the day Was thÂ» rise
to par of the Northern Pacific fours. This ia
one of a large class r.f Fimilar bonds' issued
lately for reorganization, which have been fa
vorites in the bond market. The Northern Pa
cific mortgage Is the first of them to rise to
jar. There was a very large . absorption of
other railroad bonds, which have been gaining
added f-ecurity from the largely increased,
earnings b â  rallroadÃ In the last fiscal year.

'â .'â ' ' CHICAGO- GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Aug. 3.â Higher Liverpool cables
as a result of wet weather throughout. Eng
land gave Wheat a good start here. The do
mestic receipts were still more noticeably small
than they were yesterday. That was a further
reason for strength, and as the figures were
made known during the first hour of the ses
sion an addition was made to the advance.
Chicago received only 108 cars, against 136 last
year. Business in the pit was quite active at
times, but not much of It came from outside of
Chicago There was an active â¢ demand for
Wheat for shipment East. The quotations from
Paris were at about lc a bushel reduction. This
weakness caused a break in the bullishness
here, but the market Boon revived when It be
came rumored around that the quantity sold
here for prompt shipment East amounted to
400,000 bushels. September closed %c higher
and December gained %c.
Improved prospects for the Corn crop brought
about by plenteous rains weakened Corn. Mod
erate selling by the country caused prices to
fall off early. Later, however, the Initial loss
was partly recovered -on buying against puts
and a better export demand. The close was
lie lower for September. '^ ''Â»:,'
A- good shipping demand and considerable
buying for the outside account held Oats firm.
Crop damage reports were also an influence.
September rose %<S%c on the day.
Provisions furnished a sensation on excessive
selling by B.everal of the prominent packing
interests. At one time, pork was oft 50c, lard
20c and ribs 3 c. Covering by shorts to secure
profits resulted in a reaction, but the market
received no substantial support and the feel
ing remained weak throughout the session.
Traders generally were mystified by the sud
den demoralization, but several operators/were
of the opinion that the good prospects for
ending of the war with Spain were responsible.
September pork declined 20c, lard 15c and ribs
