.DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS. " .
. The- weekly report of the Weather Bureau
says that the high , temperatures have be.en
quite .'injurious to fruits In the Sacramento and
lower San Joaquin valleys. Some varieties of
grapes, especially Tokay, have been seriously
burned. In extreme cases.' one-half the crop
has been injured. The second crop. of figs has
also been very badly Injured. â¢ Summer fruits
are ripening very rapidly, and the weather hag
been excellent, for drying. : Fruit is generally
of small size, owing to drought. The fruit con
tinues to drop from the .trees in many sec
tions; however, the unusually high prices be-
In* paid compensate for. the diminished YMÂ°:
Peaches are higher. Pears . and .Apricots are
firm. Plums are slow. . Limes are lower, The
other" descriptions range about the same. -
Prunes aTe selling in the Santa -Clara at
$35Â«40 per ton for larere- and $15^20 for the.
very email, which are in unusual mrppply this
year.

DRIED FRUITS, NUTS, RAISINS, ETC.
Interest Is centering- on Prunes at the mo
ment. Buyers are slowly advancing their bids
for the new output and have got up to 3%e
and In a few' cases to -4c for Santa Claras, but
without success, as the growers decline to eet
a selling price. Fresh Prunes are bringing
as high as $35Â®'40 per ton for the good sizes,
and Silver Prunes are bringing $60. Growers
will- probably find no difficulty in getting good
prices for dried fruit this Year. All kinds are
stiff. As the season advances are crops seem
to get smaller, owing to the widespread, drop,
from dry weather. ' â .-â 'â :Â« :â â¢'â â

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained in San Francisco for th<Â» benefit -of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation, and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 12uth meridian, by telegraphic signal re-,
celved each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, If any. Is published
In the morning papers the following day.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired), X*. S. N., In charge.
TIME BALL.
Branch Hydrogrnphlc Office. U. S. N., Mer
chants' Exchange, San Francisco, August
3. 18M.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building was dropped at exactly noon to-day â
1. c., at noon of the 120 th meridian, or at 8
p. m. Greenwich mean time.
CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired). U. S. N., In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
X T nited States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes tbe height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by' the charts. The plane of
reference Is tjie mean of the lower low waters.

WELL FED, WELL BRED.

Ulcian will always seek to put his pa-
tient on properly selected food and

.-... A food expert has discovered Grape-
Nuts by experimenting on the treat-
ment of grains to artificially pre-digest
the starches (the principal part).
Grape-Nuts as now made by the Pos-
turn Cereal Co., Lim., of Battle Creek,
Mich., and sold by grocer* generally.
Is a deliciotta duinty, suitable particu-
larly for the brain worker, as It <jnl* â¢ lc -
ly supplies the parts of food the body
needs to supply the daily loss produced
by brain work. Unless the right food
be furnished and pn perly digested,
the. individual discovers a gradual fail-
ing and some form of disease sets up.
Users of Grape-Nuts discover that
they assist in the digestion of cream
and- other food, and that a "well fed
feeliruz" follows their ut;?.

confidently claim they will go into the |
convention with enough votes from
1 Alameda County alone to nominate
' Metcalf, even if they do not get a single
| vote from the interior counties.
Jlllborn, on the other hand, returned
from Washington two weeks ago to find j
' his forces badly disorganized. His per- j
â  sonal manager. Friend, had been forced |
by his appointment as postmaster to
drop out of active politics, so the only
thing left for Hilborn to do was to seek
to form some combination, to unite his
strength with that of some other can
didate: There have been unmistakable
indications of late that such an alliance
i-s In process of formation, if, indeed, it
has not already been consummated be
: tween the present member and Pardee.
The most recent of these signs to come
; to the attention of the Metcalf forces
was a scheme to turn down C. O. Alex
! ander for the position of deputy post
master under Friend. Alexander, who
i is secretary of W. R. Davis' general
committee in charge of his guberna

Hilborn, but In reality because he was
booming Davis, to the. great mental
anguish of Pardee and the hindrance of
i the Hilborn-Pardee combination.. The
prompt exposure of the plotted down
fall of Alexander brought out a denial
; all around, but it is a noteworthy fact
that Arthur L. Martin, Democrat, still
retains the coveted deputyship.
In addition to exposing the secret
alliance with Hilborn to the Metcalf

Alexander will have the effect of in
creasing the hostility of the Davis fac
tion, and taken altogether it Is no won
der that even Pardee's friends admit
that their man is not as strong as he
was a month ago, or as they Ingeni
ously put it, that Davis has gained

DARRELL ORTH HAS
A NEW GUARDIAN
THE MOTHER'S PETITION DE-
NIED BY JUDGE HALL.
Mrs. Prescott Resigned and the Sen
sational Trial Came to an
Abrupt Termination.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Aug. 3.
Mrs. Prescott practically won In the
Darrell Orth case this morning and the
suit ended very abruptly. Yesterday a
bundle of subpenas was issued on vari
ous well-known citizens to appear at the
hearing of the case to-day, and It was
expected that some sensational matters
would be delved into.
When the case opened Judge Hall stated
that he did not care to hear any more
testimony and denied Mrs. Orth's peti
tion for a change of guardian.
The next step was taken by Mrs. Pres
cott's attorney, who asked the permis
sion of the court to file her resignation
as guardian of the child. This also was
accepted, and then on the application of
the lad's mother District Attorney Snook
was appointed his guardian. Mrs. Pree
cott seconded the proposal to make Mr.
Snook the guardian, saying that her so
ciety alwnya tried to relinquish guard
ianships in favor of reliable people.
This is a victory for Mrs. Prescott, as it
carries the presumption that the alleged
acts on her part making her an Improper
guardian were not sustained. Neither
w.i.-- the charge against her general repu
tation touched. On the other hand, Mm.
Hurt ell Orth hns gained her point by ob
taining a change of guardianship, which
Mrs. Prescott was willing from the nr>t
to grant. There is no doubt that had
the matter been fought out as bitterly
as it wan commenced much unpleasant
ness would have been engendered.

AN OVERDUE SHIP ARRIVES
Long Passage of the Henry Clement
Caused by Anti-Fouling
Paint.
The German ship Henry Clement ar
rived at Santa Rosalia on July 26 from
Hamburg. She was 235 days making the
passage, and 50 per cent reinsurance was
paid on her. In his report the captain
says the vessel's hull was coated with a
new anti-foullng paint before leaving
Hamburg, and instead of keeping the ves
sel clean it gathered every barnacle in the
Atlantic and Pacific. In consequence the
Henry Clement became unmanageable and
could only be steered when it was blow
ing a gale. Hence the, long voyage.
The new schooner Queen of the Isles,
built for the- Caroline Islands trade. Is to
be fitted with auxiliary machinery by the
Union Gas Engine Company. The en
gines are to be fifty horse power and
are expected to drive the vessel at a
speed, of ten knots. A four horse power
tender for use among the islands is also
to be put aboard the vessel. The Queen
of the Isles Is to have her trial trip on
the 10th inst., and will sail for Kusal a
few days later. Among her passengers
will be the Rev. Mr. Cannon and a num
ber (if missionaries.
â  â  The steamer Portland sailed for St.
Michael yesterday. She did not take away
many passengers. 'â ./.;
FAIR WILL CASE IN
THE SUPREME COURT
LONG ARGUMENTS HELD UPON
THE TRUST CLAUSE.
The Case Appealed From the Decision
of Judge Slack, Which De
clared the Document
Illegal.
Nearly all of the prominent lawyers In
town were present In the Supreme Court
yesterday at. the argument of the appeal
in the matter of the will of the late mil
lionaire James G. Fair. The Justices in
bank gave up the entire day to listening !
to the. attorneys. C. S. Wheeler, G. A.
Knight, J. Garber and. W. F. Herrin ap
peared for Charles. L. Fair, the contest
ant; ex-Judge Van K. Paterson and J. L.
Robison for the minor tuirs; It. 11.
Lloyd and W. S. Wood for Herman Oel
richs and wife and Miss Virginia Fair,
and R. Y. Hayne and K. B. Mitchell for

The argument was on two appealsâ one
from the decision of Judge Slack quieting
title tv realty un Montgomery street and !
the other from the order i>!" the Probate I
Court granting a partial distribution to
the children of Fair. The appeals were
virtually taken from the decree declaring
invalid 'the. -wil* of Senator Fair. In this
decree Judge Slack took the position that
the will, on account of the. trust clause,
was invalid, the clause not being recog
nized as legal by the Code. The argument
hinged upon- the last clause of the will,
which created, the trust in that it pro
vided that the remainder of the estate, I
after bequests of about '$710,000 to certain
relatives and charities, should bo Intrust
ed to three trustees, who were to have
and hold the same In tru.st during tne !
lives of the decedent's two daughters and '
â¢one son. . '
Mr. Hayne took the position that tho
â¢ trust was legal and not outside the Code,
as it was only to operate during- the lives
of the beneficiaries, and that the will
could not be legally set aside. He main
tained that the . late Senator did not
think that, his children were capable of i
managing his large property, and Bo
placed It In the hands of trustees.
Judge .Wheeler made the point that the
first paragraph of the will was a direct
conveyance of tltl*- to the. trustees and
that the testator elected' to become and
was hiÃ' own. conveyancer. The s<>l.' ques- ,
tion for the Supreme Court to deride, he !
held, was the .intention of Senator Fair,
and that If that Intention was Illegal the
will Is necessarily Invalid. Judge Pater
son argued that it was not a trust to con- â 
vey but 'Â« direct devise. The trust clause i
invalidates the will In toto.
At 430 o'clock in the afternoon tho Jus- !
tlcos decided to take the- matter under
advisement.
Irish. Soil for Fair Arrives.
The lady ami gentlemen mfmbem of the
Knights of St. Patrick met last Night at
the California Hotel to arrange for the
American booth at the Irish fair, Mrs. J.
S. Fennel presiding.
It was decided to have a children's cor
ner in the booth, where toys and knick
knacks WOUld be on sale. The handsome
flag presented by Mayor Phelan was re
ported to have been received and will be
put on exhibition at some downtown
business house before the opening of thÂ«.
fair. The society which receives the most
votes will be presented with the flag.
During the fair there will be a minuet
danced on the platform of the American
booth two evenings of the week. Ar
rangements have been made to secure
dancers and the proper instructors. The
booth will also have a supply of goods
from H. S. Crocker, which will be for
sale. Small flags will also be sold in
this booth.
On Saturday evening, August 13, O'Con
nor and Kelfeher will give an entertain
ment, the prominent feature of which will
be Irish dancing, at Golden Gate Hall,
the proceeds to be given for the fitting
out of the American booth.
The genuine Irish coil that was ordered
some time ago has arrived in this city.
Wayward Boys in Trouble.
During the month of July sixty boys
were arrested by the police of the Seven
teenth-street station on San Carlos ave
nue and Minna street, between Eighteenth
and Nineteenth. In both of those sec
tions the youngsters have become so an
noying to" the neighbors that complaints
were lodged with Captain Gillen. He de
tailed a posse of his men to arrest them.
The gang in San Carlos avenue had be
come so daring that the milkmen and
drivers of bakery wagons refused to sup
ply their customers, as the boys took de
light in stoning the unfortunate drivers,
besides raiding the wagons and carrying
away a portion of the contents. The ages
of the boys range from 12 to 20 years.

of desertion. Emma 11. Hanlon has been
granted a divorce from James A. Ilan
fon on the ground of Intemperance. Mary
D. Skinner was granted her application
for a divorce from John B. Skinner. The.
decree was Issued on the ground of willful
neglect. A dismissal has boon entered in
tin- ease of Cora B. I'lunkett against
William T. Plunkett. the well known foot
ball player and school teacher.
Fire in a Cemetery.
By the careless throwing of a lighted
cigarette among the dry grass in the
Mission Dolores Cemetery at Sixteenth
and Church streets yesterday afternoon
a fir" started which necessitated calling
cut the department. The application of
water for a few minutes succeeded in ex
tinguishing what might have been a de
structive fire. The fenc-s -surrounding
a few of the graves and the crosses
which marked the last resting-place of
some old pioneers, were burned. Father
Brennan, the pastor of the churrh. was
weii pleased that nothing more serious
resulted from the fire.

IN AID OF ST.
PATRICK'S BAZAAR
SUCCESSFUL ENTERTAINMENT j
â  AT UNION SQUARE -HALL.
Nearly Every Parish in the City Rep
resented â Coming Event at i
Odd Fellows' HalL
Another successful entertainment in aid
of St. Patrick's Bazaar was given last
evening at Union. Square Hall by the
ladles of St. Joseph's Booth under the
personal direction of Mrs. . J. Kearney.
The hall was crowded to its utmost capa
city long before the indicated time for
commencement. People from nearly every
section of the city attended in large num
bers and showed their appreciation, of the
efforts of those under whose auspices the
entertainment was given by the unusual
patronage. >'
The programme presented was entertain
ing and instructive, there being many Be
lections of unusual merit. Among â¢ its
various features was a soprano solo by
Miss Margaret Ryan and another by Miss
Frances Hausman, various character spe-
I cialties by Master Fay Doughtery and a
number of popular songs by Miss Rose
Bennt t, assisted by her sister Miss Belle
Bonnet.
The Interest and enthusiasm manifest
i ed by the general public in the coming
bazaar which will be held in the last part
of next month seem to increase as the
time for its formal opening grows near.
As it is the first time that the clergy cf
St. Patrick's Church have asked for as
sistance in making the necessary re
pairs to the old edifice on Mission street â
the fundamental object of the bazaar â
Catholics as a whole and even those out
side the faith seem to have and are tak
ing an active Interest in furthering i tho
object contemplated. Entertainments
have been given and with success, : and
other means equally efficacious financially,
have been resorted to in order to realize
the necessary money in defraying the ex
penses of arranging the preliminary work
for the coming fair. The entertainment
given last night is the last but one to bÂ»>
placed . before the charitable public be
fore the bazaar Is formally opened. . The
final one is to bo held August 19 at Old
Fellows' Hall under the auspices of the
Indies of the Charity Booth. The pro
gramme as planned will include literary,
and musical selections,- an address by
Father Gallagher of St. Ignatius Church
and a comic opera by the members of tho
Olivette Opera Company.

Tf the Southern Pacific Company shall
have provided a car for that purpose by
next week there will be established addi
tional mail facilities for the public be
tween San Francisco and San Jose. This
will give full postal car service to all of
fices between San Francisco and San Jose.
The north bound trains will make connec
tions with trains for Oregon and the
State of Washington.

PROCLAMATION.
â  STATE OF CALIFORNIA. I 1
EXECUTIVE DEPARTMENT. ]
SACRAMENTO. July 30th. IS9B. i
WHEREAS, I The Legislature of the State of i
California, at : Its thirty-second session begin- <
ning on the fourth day of January. A. D. 1597. ,
two-thirds of all the members elected to each ]
of the two houses of said Legislature voting (
in favor thereof, proposed the " following de- ,
scribed amendments to the Constitution of the
State of California, to-wit: I
AMENDMENT NUMBER ONE.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No. ,
41.) i
A resolution to propose to the people of the i
State of California, an amendment to section
eighteen , of article eleven of the Constitu-
tion, in relation to revenue and taxation, by '
which it is proposed to amend said section to
read as follows: . . .
Section 18. No county, city, town, township,
board of education, or school district. shall in- ,
cur any Indebtedness or liability in any man- ,
ncr or for any purpose exceeding in any year
the Income and revenue provided for it for â 
such year, without the assent of two-thirds
of the qualified electors thereof, voting at an
election to beh eld for that purpose, nor un-
less before, or at the time of . Incurring such
indebtedness, provision shall be made for the
collection of an annual tax sufficient to pay
the interest on such â¢ indebtedness as it falls ;
due. and also provision to constitute a- sinking
fund for the payment of 'the principal thereof
on or before maturity, which shall not exceed
forty, years from the time of contracting the :
same; provided. . however, that the City ana
â County of San Francisco may at any time pay
the unpaid claims with Interest thereon for
materials- furnished to and work done for saia <
city and county during the forty-third and ,
forty-fourth fiscal years, out of the income and
revenue of any succeeding year or years, pro-
vided, that any and all claims for making re-
pairing altering or for any work done upon or
for any material furnished for any street. . lane, |
alley, court, place or sidewalk, or forjhe con- ,
struction of any sewer or sewers In saia city ;
and county Â«re hereby excepted from the pro-
visions of this section; and in determining any
claim- permitted to be paid by this section, no
statute of limitations shall apply in any man- |
ncr; and provided further, that the City ot
Vallejo. in Solano County, may pay its exist-
ing indebtedness Incurred in the Â£?n? n , stru r t 'Â°,"
of its waterworks, whenever two-thirds of me
electors thereof voting at on election held for i
that purpose, shall so decide. Any Indebted- ,
ness or liability Incurred contrary to. this pro- j
vision, with the exception hereinbefore recuea. ;
shall be void.
AMENDMENT, NUMBER TWO.
(Being Senate Constitutional Amendment No.
10.)
A resolution proposing to the people Of the
State of California an amendment to the con-
stitution of the State, by adding a new sec-
tion, to be known and designated as section
seven and one-half, article eleven thereof pro-
viding for the framing by the inhabitants of
counties, of local county government acts tor
their own government. The said proposed new ;
section to read as follows: â¢
Section 7%. The Inhabitants of any county
may frame a county government act for their
own government, relating to the matters here-
inafter specified, and consistent with, and sub- ]
ject to. the Constitution and laws of this Male,
by' causing a board of fifteen freeholders who
have been, for at least five years qualified
electors of such county, to be elected by the |
qualified electors, of such county, at any gen-
eral or special election, whose duty it snail be j
within ninety days after such election, to pre-
pare and propose a county government act for
such county, which shall be signed In dupli-
cate by the members of such board, or a ma-
jority of them, and returned, one copy thereof
to the Board of Supervisors or other legisla-
tive body of such county, and the other copy
to be sent to the Recorder of Deeds of the
county. Such proposed county government act ,
shall then be published in two papers of gen-
eral circulation In such county, or if there be |
not two such papers, then In one only, for at |
least twenty days, and within not less than
thirty days after such publication it shall be
submitted to the qualified electors .of such j
county at a general or special election, ana lr |
a majority of such qualified electors voting .
thereon shall ratify the same It shall there- ,
after be submitted to the Legislature for â¢Us
rejection or approval, as a whole, w thout ,
power of alteration or amendment, and if ap-
proved by a majority of the members elected .
to each house, it shall be the county govern- ,
ment act of such county, and shall In such ,
case become the organic law thereof and super- f
sede any existing county government act, and
all amendments thereof, and all special laws j
inconsistent with such county government act.
A copy of such county government act. certi-
fied by the President of the Board of Super-
visors or other legislative body of such county,
pnd authenticated by the seal of such county,
totting forth, the submission of such county
' government act to the electors and its ratifica-
t ion by them, shall be made in duplicate and
deposited, one In the office of the Secretary of
State the other, after being recorded In the
office', of the recorder of deeds In the county.
I amonir the archives of the county.
â¢1! courts shall take Judicial notice thereof.
ThÂ« county government act so ratified may be
amended, at Intervals of not less than two
years by proposals therefor, submitted by the
legislative authority of the county, to the
qualified electors thereof, at a general or
special election held at least forty days after
the publication of such proposals for twenty
days in a newspaper of general circulation in
Â«uch county and ratified by at least three-
fifths of the qualified electors voting thereon,
and l approved by the Legislature as herein pro-
vided for the approval of the county . govern-
i men? act In submitting any such county gov-
e rnment act any alternative article or proposi-
tion may be presented for â¢ the choice of the
! voters? and may be voted on separately with-
1 Â° l t t P shaTi d be competent In all county govern-

TROUBLES OF
AN UMPIRE
Adventures of Charley
Sweeney at Fresno.
IS ARRESTED AND VILIFIED
OUTCOME OF DECISION AGAINST
THE HOME TEAM.
Jiaisin City Rooters Show Great Depth
of Feeling in All Matters
That Concern Their
Cluh.
BY JOE CORBETT.
At Fresno, last Sunday, the Sand Crabs
defeated the team representing the Raisin
City In a close and brilliant game, by the
score of 2 to 1.
The closeness of the contest necessitat
ed a keen sense of sagacity and a lack
of diffidence on the part of Mr. Sweeney,
â¢who was umpire. These qualities he had
In perfection, as was shown by him When,
In the last inning, he rendered a decision
which was extremely close In favor of the
Beachcombers. The decision was on a
play at the plate, and it gave the Sand
Crabs their first run, which tied the score.
Immediately after, with a man on third
base and another run needed to win the
game, Strieb hit a grounder to short stop.
Instead of throwing to the plate and en
deavoring to shut off that winning run,
this foolish short stop threw to first and
permitted the man on third to cross the
plate and win the game. So far so good.
Looks of disappointment greeted Sween
ey's rendition of the close decision at the
plate, but nothing more. The crowd
wended its way homeward, extolling the
work of the home boys, particularly that
-of Borchers, the pitcher, while the two
"teams filed their way to their different
tootles, and mind 3 r ou, nothing said about
that close decision; for both factions knew
Jt to be correct.
After a hasty bath, supper followed, and
then the usual promenade or congrega
tion In front of the hotel.
Mr. Sweeney, having nothing else to
do, strolled down the street, and with
out being previously admonished, was
Burrounded by a number of Fresno root
ers who had as their spokesman Catcher
Mangerina. Without any provocation
whatever he abuÃed ana Insulted
Mr. Sweeney. For this he received a slap
on the cheek, which was accompanied by
the remark: "If you were not so small
I would lick yon good," and adding, "if
'there is any one in the crowd who would
like to take this up 1 will accommodate
him."
It is needless to mention that they per
mitted Sweeney's challenge to pass un
noticed and dispersed. 1 am informed by
an observer that the rapidity of their
flight was indescribable.
The telegraphic account of this mix
up was Incorrect, stating as it did that
Sweeney knocked Mangi rina down. He j
simply slapped him. and for the trutn of
this statement I have the word of the en
tire Santa Cruz team, including its man-
ager.
Just as soon as this fight* or, rather,
chastisement occurred. Manager "Warde
of the Fresno team swore out a warrant
for Sweeney's arrest, and without a i
chance for an explanation he was put in
jail and his bail fixed at $250. Consterna
tion and confusion ensued for a brief pe
riod, as that amount of money was not j
to be had among the Santa Cruz play
ers or its manager. A prominent citi
zen of the town came forward, however,
and advanced the necessary amount.
Sweeney was allowed to go free with a
wink from the Judge that all was well.
He was also told to appear next morning
for trial, but this as a precaution, for he
was assured by his bondsman that it ;
was perfectly proper for hir-^to leave j
town with the Santa Cruz team. In ac
cordance with this advice Sweeney joy
fully accepted the proposition to leave
town, and boarded the first train for
this city in company with the Sand
Crabs.
On the way the deplorable and un
sportsmanlike behavior of the Fresno
manager was freely discussed.
When far into Merced County Tutle was
awakened by a severe tugging at his â¢
Bide. Angrily turning over and wonder- j
Ing who would have the impudence to
mar his rest, he was informed that the
disturber and tormenler was no other
than the constable of Newman, at which
town the train had stopped. On hearing j
the name "constable," compliance with
any old request v,-;us his first thought, and I
when told that orders had been received '
from Fresno to take into custody a Mr. |
Sweeney, Mr. Tutle was amazed, lie re- i
covered shortly, and directed the gentle- i
man to Sweeney's berth, which was dl- !
rectjy under his own.
It took but a second to- explain the Bit- j
uation to Sweeney. He was accused of !
jumping his bondÃ and must return to
Fresno. Explanations were unavailing,
bo he had to leave the train. He attempt
ed to dress himself, but time was precious j
with both railroad men and constable, j
and In consequence he was compelled to j
make a hasty retreat into the town of
Newman.
The train having 1 pulled away, and with
It all chance of immediate escape, a
dressing-room was sought in a neighbor-
Ing saloon.
The constable turned out to be a jovial !
chap, and was asked by Sweeney to have ]
a drink. Nothing suited his taste better,
and so it went on. Sweeney treating, then
the constable, then the saloon-keeper.
Believing Sweeney to be "all right,' as
he expressed it. he said "good-night."
adding that Sweeney must be on hand at
6 a. m.
This gentleman's recollections of Fresno
hospitality being fresh in his mind, do- j
elded that escape, and at once, was ex
pedient. A hurried conference was held
with the saloonkeeper as to the quick
est way to San Francisco, and It was
found that but one avenue of escape of
fered itself, and this included a long
tramp over the railroad tries to the town
of Tracy. "Anything other than a return
to Fresno" was (Sweeney's motto, and
the following story of his escape I re
ceived from him: "I started on my long
tramp for Tracy at about 3:30 a. m. and
arrived there lute in the morning. Hav
ing very little change, and not knowing
what to do, I walked up to the depot and
Inquired of a young lady in the office
what time the first train left for Stock
ton. I was informed tha*. the only train
for some time was a freight, and fearing
recapture told her that 1 was a drummer
and had fallen off the early train and
requested that she telegraph Immediately
to headquarters for an order which
would enable me to take the expected
freight train for home as I was Internally
injured and needed skilled medical at
tendance. I must have charmed her with
my story for she set to work and short-
Sy after came my order to board the
freight. This I did In due time and on
arriving at Stockton caught the first
train for the city."
go ended the trouble of an umpire.
What! oh. what: is this baseball world
coming to when an umpire dare not g\vi
a. decision against the home tc-um without
endangering his future prospects?

RED MEN CHOSE THEIR GREAT CHIEFS
G. W. Collins Elected Great Sachem and Mrs.
Hattie A. Whipple Great Pocahontas.
At the second day's session of the Great Council of the Improved Order
of Red Men. held yesterday, the forty-seven tribes of the reservation
were represented by 200 delegates.
The principal business was the election of great chiefs for the ensuing
term The following was the result: F. A. Whipple of No. 60, Fort
Brags. Kreat prophet ; George W. Collins of No. 77, San Francisco, great
sachem; B. B. Wilson of No. 14, Sacramento, great senior sagramore; F.
D Brandon of No. 11, San Francisco, great junior sagamore; C. F. Burg
man of No. 57. great chief of records; William J. Smith of No, 4, great
keeper of wampum; George W. I-ovie of No. 24, Redwood City, F. A.
Whipple of No. 60, Fort Bragg, and C. F. Burgman of No. 57. San Francisco,
great representatives to the Great Council of the United States; William
Rotrosky of No. 0. Henry A. Chase of No. 4 and Benjamin F. Josselyn of
No 12 all of San Francisco, grand trustees.
The Great Council of the United States will meet in Indianapolis next
The Grent Council offered a prtze of $60 for the best rendition of the
adoption degree, and a prize of $100 for the best rendition of the warrior
and chief degrees.
To-night Oshcmee Tribe will give a grand ball and entertainment In
ShieN building, to which all the representatives have been invited.
The committee that was appointed to judge of the excellence of the
work by the teams of the Alfarata and Yosemite councils of the Degree of
Pocahontas in the exemplification of the adoption degree decided yester
day in favor of Alfarata Council, and to it was awarded, as prizes, three
silver tomahawks, $10, and five pounds of candy.
A great portion of the time of the Great Council of the Degree of Po
cahontas; wns devoted to the amending of the constitution, and all amend
ments were referred to the committee on laws and supervision.
The following were then elected chiefs for the ensuing term: Mrs.
Fannie Lovie of Redwood City, great prophetess; Mrs. Hattie A. Whipple
of Fort Bragg, great pocahontas; Mrs. Annie E. Bishop of Alnmeda, great
wenonah; Mrs. L. Bean, great minnohaha; Mrs. E. J. Brazile, great
keeper of records; Mrs. A. E. Gill, groat keeper of wampum: Mrs. B. Mc-
Kenna, Mrs. Emma Fiske and Mrs. E. Rohrbacher, trustees.
The following are the appointed officers: Mrs. Jennie Weisel. great first
scout: Mrs. E. I,ubeck. great second . scout; Mrs. E. M. McLane. great
guard of the tepee, and Mrs. Edith Gish. great guard of the wigwam.
Mrs. Nora Klenk. Mrs. H. Gutstadt and Mrs. A. E. Bishop were elected
a committee on revision of the laws of the subordinate councils.
In the evening there was a grand bull in Native Sons' Hall. It was
termed "the great sun dance in honor, of the great sun session of IS9S,"
and was well attended. There was a line programme of dances, and all
who took part greatly enjoyed themselves. . , :
TELEGRAPH OVER
CHILKOOT PASS
WTBE WILL CONNECT JTJNEATJ
AND CIRCLE CITY.
Canadian Government Grants a
Franchise to a San Francisco
Corporation, Which Will
Push the Work.
After a strong and vigorous opposition
on the part of the Canadian Pacific Rail
way, the Alaska Telegraph and Telephone
Company, composed of prominent San
Francisco capitalists, has succeeded in se
curing a franchise from the Canadian
Government granting them the right to
construct, operate and maintain telegraph
and telephone lines in Canadian territory
over the Chilkoot Pass, running along the
lakes and up the Lewis and Yukon rivers
to Dawson City.
In return for the granting to them of a
franchise the Canadian Government is to
have free access to the use of the wires
of the company without any charge there
for. Â â¢
The line will begin at Juneau. Alaska,
and from there will run in a northerly
direction to the town of Dyea, thence, in
a north^asterlv direction over the Chil
koot Pass. It "will then follow the line of
tie Ivcwis nnc Yukon rivers to Dawson
City, and from there on to Circle City,
where it will terminate.
The company was incorporated, in this
city in July, 1897, for $250,000, divided into
25 000 share's of the par value of $10 each.
The total amount paid in. is $10,000. '
The officers of the company are Theo
dore Reichert, president, and J. F. Fas
sett secretary. . The present directors
are: I. J. Truman, of the Columbian
Bunking Company; Theodore Reichert.
late Surveyor General of the State; D. E.
Bohannan, George T. Gaden and J. F.
Fassett.
It is the intention of the company to
push the work of construction with the
greatest rapidity possible. The estimated
length of the line and its branches is said
to be about 1000 miles.
Stmr. Grace Dollar will sail for St. Michael.
Guaranteed connections Yukon River points;
low rates. Particulars 46 Mkt. or 632 Mkt.. r. 8.
Dogs as a Nuisance.
A. A. Newbert, 3443 Eighteenth street,
swore to a complaint in Judge Mogan's
court yesterday for the arrest, of William
Ryan, his next-door neighbor, for main
alning a nuisance. Ryan owns a pack
of greyhounds, and Newbert and other
residents in the neighborhood say that
they cannot Bleep at nights for the howl
ing" of the dogs.

AN UPRISING
IS EXPECTED
Chinese Provinces Are
in a Ferment.
FOOD IS VERY SCARCE
G. E. SEVEY TELLS OF IM
PENDING MASSACRES.
There Is Great Danger to the Mis
sionaries in Northern and
Central China.
G. E. Sevey, president of the American
Inland. Steam Navigating Company, op
erating towing launches on the Grand j
canal of China, arrived on th* Doric on
Tuesday and brings with him interesting
news from the northern and central
provinces of China. He ,â  has traveled
through those districts and possesses
a thorough knowledge of the social and
political conditions now existing through- ;
out the Chinese empire.
He fears a violent outbreak among the ;
hordes of Chinese in both the northern
and central provinces and conscientious
ly believes that a great loss of life ami
destruction of property will result. no
missionaries scattered throughout this
country, he thinks, will be the chief suf- ;
f 6rcrs
"There has been a drought in Northern \
and Central China," he said yesterday at
the Occidental Hotel, "and there will be
trouble before i long In both those dis
tricts. The rainy season in China is in
May and June, but this year there- was ;
little rain. The rice crops, which the
people depend upon for subsistence, will
be so scant and meager that destitution
is certainly impending for millions of na- ;
tives. The poorer classes have been ,
squeezed by the mandarins and Chinese ;
governors to such an extent that noth
ing is left them now but mere existence.
This year there will.be nothing for the :
governors to draw out of the people. ;
"There are several, thousand American
missionaries working in Northern and
Central China and when the uprising
comes, as I think it will, for these fam
ine-stricken- Chinese will fight for food,
they "will be one of the first to suffer.
The* American missionaries . are well
treated in China, especially by the poorer
classes.but when a Chinese population bo
comes mad in their rage for food they
level all distinctions and set about sweep
ing everything before them. They are a
rabble without a head.
"In Chinking placards about the town
warned foreigners to escape the Impend
ing wrath of the mad populace. But no
riot occurred. In the latter part of June
of this year Yang Chow was placarded. ,
Warnings were tacked to the mission
aries' gates informing them of- a. pros
pective outbreak. This has been done all
through the Yangtse Valley district. In
\Vuhu an outbreak was threatened, -but
the English Governor, acting for the Chi
nese Government, dared the mob. He fur
nished his troops with rifles and is wait-
Ing for any attack that might come. He
In turn threatened . utter destruction to
the Chinese discontents should they at- ;
tempt any violence." . I
G F Sevey was the first operator of
any steam craft on the Grand canal.
He* has been in China for a number of
years, with headquarters at Shanghai.

raphy It would surprise him more than
any one else.

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for repaying and repairing
streets paved with bitumen. Office of the
Clerk of the Board of Supervisors of the
City and County of San Francisco, August
.2. IS9B. Â£ â  â  \ . â â ...,
In accordance with Resolution No. 1438
(Fourth Series) of the Board or Supervisors,
sealed proposals will be received In open
session of the Board on Monday afternoon,
August 8, 1898. from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for
repaying, repairing and keeping In good order
and condition, to the entire satisfaction of.
the . Superintendent of . Streets and of the
Board of Supervisors, all streets or portions
of streets, Including- street crossings and in-
tersections, which are now or may bo here-
after paved with bituminous rock, from date
of award of contract to and Including 'June
80, ISOO.
The repaying and repairs are. to be made in
strict accordance with specifications on tile
and open for inspection in this office. .
The streets and street crossings, etc.. now
paved with bituminous rock, and which will
require to be repaved and kept In repair, as
above stated, include all streets or portions of
streets paved with bituminous rock and ac-
cepted by the city and county, and must be
put in good condition and kept In thorough
repair. All streets and street crossings enu-
merated In the specifications to be repaved
must be so repaved within a period of three
(3) months after the contract Is awarded, and
all streets, street crossings and street Inter-
sections paved with bitumen must be put In
good repair and condition within a period of
three months from award of contract, and
must be kept in good repair until the expira-
tion of the contract; in addition to all streets
or portions of streets â¢ paved with bituminous
rock and accepted by the city anil county dur-
ing the continuance of the contract, except
only such streets or portions of streets where
a bond . has been given and is in force for
keeping the same in repair; also, those por-
tions of streets required by law to be kept In
repair by the street railway companies hav-
ing tracks thereon.
NOTlCEâ Bidders must state a specific sum
for which the entire work will be performed,
with the understanding that monthly pay-
ments will be made therefor to the extent of
one twenty-fourth of the sum bid, on a cer-
tificate from the Superintendent of Streets,
approved by the Board of Supervisors, that
the work Is being satisfactorily performed.
Bidders will also be required to file with
their bid a description of and the character
of the plant owned by them, with a chemi-
cal analysis and a sample of the bituminous
rock' to be used in carrying out and per-
forming the work under this contract.
And It Is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
persons employed on the above work, and
that the minimum rate of wages to be paid
shall be two ($2) dollars per day; also, that
the amount of the bond to be given will not
be lews than one-half the amount bid for the
performance of this work.
No bid will be received or entertained from
any person, firm or corporation who does not
own a fully equipped plant sufficient for the
performance of this work. - â  r- ;"
The party to whom the contract Is awarded
will be required, prior to or at the time of
the execution of said contract, to pay the cost
of advertising this and a former notice In
three daily newspapers.
In order to preserve uniformity and to facil-
itate the award the Board has resolved to
receive no bid unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the committee, and a certified
check . for - the sum of five thousand ($3000)
dollars In favor of and made payable to the
Clerk of the Board, must-be deposited by the
bidder, conditioned, if the proposal Is accept-
ed and the contract awarded, and if the bid-
der shall, fail or neglect to execute a written
agreement and give the bond required (with
two (2) good and sufficient 'sureties, each to
qualify in the full amount of . said bond,
which bond shall not be for a sum less than
one-half the contract price) within â¢ six (6)
days after the award is made, then, ; and In
that case, the said sum shall be paid Into the
City and County .Treasury by said Clerk as
liquidated' damages for such failure and neg-
lect. ;.-- >â â  â¢â â¢â¢ ' ' . ; ' - , â - V .
Blanks furnished by the Clerk., -
The Board reserves the right to reject ail
bids if the public good so require.
- JNO. A. RUSSELL. Clerk.

SOCIAL GAUGE
A Bouillon Cup for the Fair
Comedienne's Escut
cheon.
Madeline Bouton Drinks Grotto Soup While
Miss Tinnie Sips Her Broth in the
Grillroom.
The breach In the Frawley Company Is
widening. Social conditions have crept
into the- melee. Hastily improvised es
cutcheons are being brought to the front
and aspersions are exist as to the secur
ity of the marble staircase that leads to
the Grotto. Tho vital point now to be
determined is. Which Is the more aris
tocraticâto sip your broth in the grill
room or to stay a weak and . fasting
Btomach .with nourishing Grotto soup?
Otherwise T. Daniel and his interesting
family are very well, thank you, with, of
course, the exception of Miss Madeline
Bouton, who' still occupies a cot In the
itfcNutt Hospital, suffering with an af
fection of the spine.
Miss Bouton was resting more easily
yesterday, and was strong enough to con
fide to a Cull- representative her mortifi
cation and unhapplness at the published
account of her dismissal from the Fraw
ley Company and the. association of her
name In the same column with that of
her sister artiste, Cora Tinnle.
To Frawley's erstwhile leading lady the
fair Cora is "that Tinnie woman," and
the very mention of the Tinnle name
Cora Tinnie and Some Suggestions for Escutcheon
Designs for the Warring Actresses.
makes Miss Bouton remember that- she
-the dsshing. sparkling Madelineâ has a
social position to maintain, and which
she must not jeopardize by Tinnie asso
ciations. PrevfoOs to yesterday Miss Tin
nie revvled in blissful unconsciousness of
social distinctions, and hatl not even
glv^n a thought to the dividing Bouton
rinnie line. However, when the matter
tvas railed to her attention sha puckered
tier brow and thought for a brief moment
a. deep, hard thought.
"Now that I come to consider It, there
s a difference," she said. "I gauge it by
i cup of broth. I take my cup of bouillon
;h the grill room. Miss Bouton drinks
Her soup in the Grotto, and the stairs are
?o treacherous; especially for one who is
jiven to falling."
Apropos of falls Miss Tinnie does not
-emember the fall at rehearsal, the pri
mary cause of Miss Boutori'B illness.
The story of tho sick actress 1 dismissal
'rom the Frawley Company still agitates
:he minds of her numerous admirers. The
ictress denies it, despite the fact of eye
witnesses who saw the leading lady re

Cuticura Cured. Skin Now Clear
A SWORN STATEMENT.
"When my nephew was two weeks old he had
running sores on hia face, neck, breast, and
hands, which continued to grow worse, They
looked like raw beef. Sometimes blood would
come and sometimes matter. He was awfully
bad. Why, we had never heard or read of
ench a case. My sister had to tie his hands to
the hammock to. keep him from scratching
himself, he itched so. He often nibbed his
neck and face against his shoulders. The
doctor did little or no good. Said "it came
from the blood," etc. She heard of Cctti-
cura (ointment), and I bought her a box, and
finding it gave satisfaction she continued to
use it. The boy is ail icell now and Aw face ia
clear. In order to show you my good faith in
this matter and how pleased I am'with Ctrn-
ccba Rkmediks. I have taken oath to above
Statements. EMMA FISHER, 6B2 W. 18th Pl. t

"I was not dismissed," Miss Bouton
said; "the report Is as absurd and unjust
as it Is untrue. Why, Mr. Frawley and
the rest of the management are more
than kind to me, and the very best of
feeling exists between us. It was at Mr.
Frawley's earnest request that I. came
to the sanitarium. Anxious to see me re
stored to health, with unparalleled gener
osity, my manager offered to pay my ex
penses, at the hospital."
And: so it Is only on account of Air. Fraw
ley and his associates that Miss Bouton
is anxlotis to see the report of her dis
missal denied.
Respect for Frawley Is a strong point
with Miss Bouton. She seems to have an
ardent desire to keep her thoughts in his
direction. On the snowy coverlid of the
bed on which she tosses lies a letter from
Frawley. On the table at her side repose

TIIE SA3T FRAKCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, AUGUST 4, 1898.
DISTURBED
THE BURGLAR
AT HIS WORK
Police Looking for a
Sleek Schemer.
ALLEGED THEATRICAL MAN
ENGAGED A ROOM IN A BUSH
STREET HOUSE.
The Landlady Returns With Friends
in Time to Prevent the Thief
From Looting the
Place.
The police are Investigating the extraor
dinary actions of C. 11. Nelson, alias C.
H. Fullerton, a young man who repre
sented himself to be a theatrical man, but
\ they have reason to believe that he Is a
i notorious crook, who would not stop at
| murder to accomplish his ends.
Lust Friday night he called at the lodg
iriK-house of Mrs. Russell, 616 Bush street,
and told her that .he came from Sacra
mento to accept an engagement at the
Tivoli, and as he had been recommended
by a friend, whose name he had forgot
ten, to put up at her house, he would be
I glad if she could accommodate him. He
! only had a hand satchel with him, but
explained that his "luggage" would come
later. He paid his first week's room
J rent, and soon made himself at home.
Saturday afternoon he gave Mrs. Rus
sell three tickets for the Orpheum, and
asked her to take the other two ladies-
in tho house with her. Owing to the re
cent death of her husband, who was
killed at Seattle, Mrs. Russell told him j
she could not go, and finally Mrs. Wade,
one of the other ladies, said she would !
get the tickets changed for Sunday night, j
which was done.
Mrs. Dora Kellerman, who occupies the
suite of rooms on the ground floor, found
Sunday morning that an attempt had
been made to force open the door of her j
room. The key had been In the door, and '
It had been twisted by the efforts to j
turn It. That afternoon Mrs. Russell j
went to Holy Cross Cemetery to visit the j
grave of her husband, and about 5
o'clock Nelson knocked on Mrs. Wade's
door, and when she opened it he apolo- j
glxed and said he thought it was Mrs.
Russell's room. His object evidently was
to locate Mrs. Russell's room, but Mrs.
Wade did not gratify his curiosity.
All the roomers had left the house by
8 o'clock Sunday night, except a Mr. Fel- j
ton, who occupies a room on the top floor. !
Mrs. Russell, after returning from the ;
cemetery, met a lady friend. Mrs. Gratto,
and a gentleman friend of her late hus- i
band, and went and had dinner With
them. They accompanied her home about ,
half-past 8 o'clock. Mrs. Russell, on go- ;
ing upstairs, hoard a peculiar noise in the
bath room, and on opening the door
found Nelson there. He made some lame j
excuse and disappeared from the house, i
taking his hand satchel with him.
When Mrs. Kellerman returned she
found hor trunk wide open. An examlna- I
tion showed that the lock had been forced ,'
open with a "Jimmy," and the contenrg
had b<;en turned over for money or jew- '
elry. She had- fortunately taken her j
purse and jewelry- with her. It was evi
dent that Nelson had been disturbed m !
bis work on the trunk and had slipped j
out of the bedroom door and upstairs
when he heard Mrs. Russell and her j
friends return to the house. He had lc?s
than half an hour to do his work In.
Mrs. Russell lias a valuable lot of jew- !
elry. and that was possibly knovn to j
Nelson.
Nelson Is described as about 5 feet 8 '
inches tall, about 23 years of age, smooth !
shaven, dark complexion, and wore a !
Prince Albert coat, dark trousers and i
black dicer. He was very pleasant In his
conversation.
DEATH CLAIMS ATTORNEY W. F. GOAD.
The Prominent Lawyer and Financier Passes Away Suddenly
From an Attack of Apoplexy.
William F. Goad, for many years closely identified with the legal, so
cial and business Interests of this city, died unexpectedly at Hotel Rafael
yesterday morning. Apoplexy was the direct cause of his death. He suf
fered a stroke of the dread disease at 3 o'clock in the morning and sank
gradually until the end came at 11. Decedent had been ill for some clays
but no serious outcome was expected. He did not come to the city on
Monday, although at that time he did not require the attention of his phy
sician. It was decided by the members of his family that ifhewasnotim
proved yesterday the doctor would be summoned. He could render no
assistance at the end.
Decedent was a Kentuckian by birth and proved a marked success as a
financier and man of affairs, as well as In his chosen profession of the
law. He was at one time vice president of Wells Fargo & Co.'s Ex
press. He was president of the Bank of Haywards; a director of the Pa
cific Bell Telephone Company and a director of the Bank of Willows and
the Bank of Colusa. In politics he was a Democrat. He served a term
as school director In the city and was a candidate for the Mayoralty In
1890, being defeated by Georgo H. Sanderson.
Socially his family is widely known. It consists of Mre. Osgood
Hooker, Mrs. Charles Mclntosh, Miss Genevieve Goad and W. F. Goad
Jr. He wan a member of the Pacific-Union and of. the University Club
and was Identified vtlth their development.
DISASTROUS
ENDING OF A
GOLDEN DREAM
Collapse of the Klondike Boom.
Hits Hard the Humboldt's
Charterers.
Baron yon Schroeder Loses Heavily in the
Venture and Is Now Glad to Be Rid
of the Steamer.
The steamer Humboldt has been with
drawn from the Alaskan trade and the
people who chartered her are now wish
ing they had never heard of the gold
fields on the banks of the Yukon. Among
the sufferers Is the Baron yon Schroeder.
He Is said to be out to the extent of $100,
000 on the venture, but then the money
cannot be counted a total loss as his ship
ping experience, acquired while handling
! a "Klondike rush," is surely worth
something. The Baron went Into the
scheme with his eyes open, but now, ac
! cording to the water front, he knows a
j thing or two about steamboating that he
! never dreamed of before. Experienco
comes high in that line and the Baron's
cost him $100,000.
A year ago the rush to Dawson was at
its height. Men and women, too, were
tumbling over each other In the mad rush
i to reach the new El Dorado. All kinds of
vessels were pressed into service and
craft that had been In retirement for
years were pulled out of the different
creeks and put on the Dyea and Skaguay
runs. Coasters were hauled off th>ir
! regular routes and steam schooners were
| turned into passenger vessels. Colliers
haÂ«l bunks put In tfieir 'tween decks and
' steamers that had been used in the cat
| tie trade were fixed up to carry gold hunt
ers. A dozen Eastern steamers came
around the Horn to Join In the rush, while
in expectation of the spring stampede via
St. Michael and the Yukon the keels of
fully a hundred river boats were la;d and
1 ocean-going steamers were taken ofc their
regular voyages because bigger money
could be earned carrying passengers for
Of the steamers chartered at a high fig
ure the Australia was one and the Hui"
boldt another. Both were secured at $800
a day, but when the bottom began drop
' ping" out of the boom the Pacific Coast
I Steamship Company was able to cancel
| its charter of the Australia, but the char
terers of the Humboldt were not po ior- |
[ tunate. Under the terms of their con
tract they are bound to keep the vessel
for nine months at the rate of $24,000 a
month and only two-thirds of the time
has elapsed. Arrangements have now
been made, however, by which a certain
sum of money changes hands and the ves
sel Is returned to her owners.
The ostensible charterer of thP Hum
boldt was J. A. Magee Jr., but the back
bone of the venture was the Baron yon
Schroeder. An Ironclad agreement was
entered Into with the Humboldt Steam
ship Company after a great deal of bick
ering. Other people were after the
steamer so the owners set a figure and
would not abate a cent. Finally the
price was agreed upon, the Baron fur
nished a gilt-edged bond for the due per
formance of his part of the contract
and the Humboldt was turned over to the
n<:\v management.
Had the boom continued all would
have bern well, ?s the Humboldt is one
of the fastest and best equipped steam
ers on the coast. During her term of
service on the Klondike run she has
made more and faster trips than any other
vessel in the business, while the num
ber of passengers she carried has been
above the average. Still, she did not
carry enough to pay, and that was Boon
self-evident. However, the vessel was
kept going, and as each month came
around the sum of $24,000 was duly hand
ed over to the owners of the steamer,
while the charterers said nothing but
lived In hopes that business would pick
up with the opening of the Yukon River
in the spring.
In order to be ready for the rush to
Dawson via St. Michael and the Yukon,
the charterers of the Humboldt began
building two stern wheel steamers on the
Columbia River. These were named the
Came Cock and the Stag Hound. While
passing out over the river bar on their
way north the vessels were badly strain
ed, and on top of that a storm was en
countered outside. Both of them near
ly went to the bottom, and It was only
with the greatest difficulty that their
captains got them back to Astoria. They
now lie on the beach there of no use
to anybody while the owners and under
writers are fighting over them In the
The war with Spain opened before the
iukon River and the prospective gold
hunters enlisted in the army instead of
taking passage to the gold fields. The
Humboldt s first and only voyage to St.
Michael was made at even a greater loss
than was the last one to Dyea and
bkaguay, so Baron yon Schroeder decid
ed that it was time to wind up the busi
ness and get out. This he has done and
is now a poorer but wiser man.

Aydelotte's Business College.
OAKLAND, Aug. 3.â The popular
business and shorthand Institute of
Oakland has moved from the Y. M. C.
A. building back to Broadway and is
having a remarkable run of business.
The school has had a most prosperous
season since the first of January, but
the rush of business at the new location
in the Kohler & Chase block, Broadway
and Eleventh, is beyond all expectation
and all precedent for the month of Au
gust. The college moved at the close
of last week and was ready for busi
ness Monday morning, the Ist of Au
gust. .
Professor Aydelotte, the president and
manager of the institution, which is a
corporation, reports that there have
been more entries the first three days
of August this year than during the
entire month of August last year. The
friends and patrons of this local insti
tution who have watched its steady and
substantial growth for the past ten
years are glad to hear of its prosperity.
Nearly two hundred students have en
rolled since the first of January.
Alice Rix Writes About the
Princess Who Wanted to Be Queen
In Next Sunday's Call.
E. CTTLLEN ASPHYXIATED.
He Has Been 111 and Out of Work
for a Month, and Grew De
spondent.
Edward Cullon, a native of California,
about 32 years old, was found dead In bed
yesterday by his mother and his sister In
tho family residence, 2707 Vallejo. street.
The gas Jet had been turned on. The
family say that water In tho pipe or a
sudden decrease In the pressure extin
guished the light after he went to sleep
on the evening before and that when the
pressure was put on again or when the
obstruction was removed' the gas escaped
and asphyxiated the young man.
Decedent had been. lll with rheumatism
for about. a month, but apparently had no
Intention of committing suicide, as his
family Is well to do. It Is likely that a
suit for damages will be filed against the
gas company for reducing the pressure
and then turning It on again.
